		 			Praise for Shared Wisdom

Revised and Expanded 20th Anniversary Edition

			“Shared Wisdom, over the years, has proven to be an indispensable resource for chaplains, spiritual care providers, therapists, and clinical pastoral education educators. Using case study as a pedagogical method to highlight the intersubjective nature of caring relationships, this new and updated edition offers the reader a fuller and more nuanced understanding of who we are, who others are, who God is, and the shared wisdom that arises in spiritual care relationships. It is an essential text for theological schools preparing students for the reflective practice of spiritual care within the context of complex interreligious realities.”

			—Dr. Su Yon Pak, vice president of academic affairs and dean, Union Theological Seminary, and coauthor of Sisters in Mourning: Daughters Reflecting on Care, Loss, and Meaning

			“This updated edition is a must-read for all spiritual care practitioners as they strive to provide effective, relevant, quality care. Through case studies, Pamela Cooper-White identifies common themes and dynamics in today’s world (for example, gender and racial equity, religious pluralism) that require the caregiver’s commitment to crafting healthy responses through self-reflection, self-monitoring, and self-analysis.”

			—Bishop Teresa E. Snorton, BCC, ACPE (retired); Ecumenical and Program Development Officer, The CME Church; and coeditor of Women Out of Order: Risking Change and Creating Care in a Multicultural World

			“This book is a must-read to understand in-depth and work with the shared wisdom generated in therapeutic relationships and in spiritual care in particular. Pamela Cooper-­White has birthed an improved classic: gracefully crafted with compelling insight and generous guidance for competent praxis.”

			—Daniel S. Schipani, author of Spiritual Care in Our Multifaith World: A Primer on Practice and Theory

			“With twenty years of additional scholarship and fresh insight, Pamela Cooper-White provides helpers of all kinds with new ways of thinking about the unconscious dynamics of the therapeutic relationship. Her insight is especially keen regarding unconscious enactments by therapists, chaplains, and spiritual caregivers who may view their behavior toward clients simply as care and empathy, failing to fully appreciate the narcissism and grandiosity inherent in the role of the helper. For all who find themselves in the role of the one who helps, read and take heed. There is valuable advice in the pages of this new volume.”

			—Mary Ragan, PhD, LCSW, senior staff therapist at Psychotherapy and Spirituality Institute, New York City

			“In her revised and expanded edition, Pamela Cooper-White has given us a window into how the evolving nature of pastoral care and counseling is shaped by cultural shifts, such as attending to religious experiences beyond Christianity and heteronormativity, but also how the theologian is changed by deep pastoral listening and theological reflection. And we witness this shift throughout the work in the nuanced and substantive ways she engages theory and lived experiences. The expanded version has the depth we have come to appreciate from Cooper-White and challenges us anew to further our understanding and vision of pastoral care and counseling. Required reading for serious students and colleagues.”

			—Phillis Isabella Sheppard, E. Rhodes and Leona B. Carpenter Chair of Religion, Psychology, and Culture, and executive director of the James Lawson Institute for the Research and Study of Nonviolent Movements, Vanderbilt University

			“Pamela Cooper-White has written a book that everyone in the field of spiritual care needs to read. Her insights help the reader develop essential reflective practices for exploring how their selves interact with the people they care for and the unique ethics of embracing the richness of intersubjectivity in a world filled with diverse identities and points of view. Her theological and spiritual vision lifts up the essential role that spiritual care plays in supporting transformative and healing relationships in a broken world. Shared Wisdom is a gift.”

			—Rabbi Mychal B. Springer, ACPE, BCC, manager of clinical pastoral education, Department of Spiritual Care and Chaplaincy, New York-Presbyterian Hospital

			“The twentieth-anniversary revised and expanded edition of Pamela Cooper-­White’s Shared Wisdom: Use of the Self in Pastoral Care and Counseling adds depth and breadth to an already excellent resource. This volume offers a clear and historically grounded description of the relational model of care, elucidating the way that shared wisdom emerges in and through intersubjective caregiving relationships. Through multicultural case studies and pastoral theological reflection, the author teaches pastoral caregivers how to become aware and make use of the unconscious processes that arise in their caregiving relationships. This edition is enhanced by a greater emphasis on social justice as it relates to healing, as well as increased attention to religious pluralism.”

			—Mary Clark Moschella, Roger J. Squire Professor of Pastoral Care and Counseling, Yale University School of Divinity

Preface


			 			In the fields of pastoral care and counseling, we have long known that our own subjectivity as religious leaders, chaplains, counselors, and psychotherapists is central to our ability to be present and respond empathically to those who come to us for care and support. Classical training in recognizing our “own stuff” has prepared us to examine the unfinished business of our own lives so that our unresolved issues would not distort or interfere with the healing potential of our pastoral relationships. At least subliminally, however, most of us have known that more is going on in the pastoral relationship, and in our subjective experience of those who come to us for help, than just a polarity of “their stuff” and “my stuff.” The spiritual caregiving relationship involves intersubjectivity, a sharing of understandings and meanings that arises in the “potential space” of exploration between us.1

			There is a shared wisdom that grows and is held between helper and helpee2 in the pastoral relationship, and this shared wisdom exists in both conscious and unconscious dimensions of “I,” “Thou,” and “We.” As we engage deeply and compassionately with the other, we find that we draw wisdom about that individual’s perspective and experience from the shared exploration of meanings that arise between us and are cocreated uniquely in this pastoral relationship. How do we come to discern this shared wisdom?

			The purpose of this book is to show that by delving into our own subjective experience of the counseling relationship, known in the helping professions as our countertransference, we will come to a deeper, more empathic appreciation of the other and be more open to the other’s own thoughts, feelings, insights, and hopes for growth and healing. The book will lead the reader through a method for pastoral assessment and theological/spiritual reflection that makes use of the pastoral caregiver’s own self as a primary tool for discernment and ultimately for praxis. I deliberately use the term praxis rather than practice because “praxis,” drawn from critical theory, “is defined as action or engagement upon the world that seeks to create change.”3 As the case examples throughout this volume will show, social context is an important foundation for understanding others’ needs—both conscious and unconscious. The social and political surround is always present, and attentiveness to the ways in which social justice is a part of the larger dimension of healing is needed—even when engaged in one-on-one care.

			This method will involve several dimensions: spiritual contemplation and centering self-care; an examination of what is happening in one’s own thoughts, feelings, impulses, and experiences; theological/spiritual reflection, leading to a deeper assessment of the needs of the other; and ultimately a pastoral praxis that is based on the shared wisdom of both partners in the pastoral relationship. In addition, the book will help readers to become clearer about their boundaries as helping professionals, recognizing that the ethics of care become at the same time more important and more comprehensible when professionals understand the intersubjective nature of the caring relationship.

			The book consists of three main sections. The introduction and first two chapters trace the pastoral relationship, and particularly an understanding of the concept of countertransference through its historic context. These chapters place this relationship within a larger framework of postmodern understandings of how we come to know another person.

			The larger portion of the book, chapters 3 through 5, presents a method of pastoral assessment and theological/spiritual reflection based on this use of the pastoral self in congregation-based pastoral care, in chaplaincy, and in pastoral counseling and psychotherapy. In many ways, this process belongs to a larger methodological turn toward reflective practice as articulated by practical theologians such as Don Browning, Richard Osmer, and Thomas Groome.4 These methods all revolve around a circular movement from theory to practice to theory.5 Emmanuel Lartey expanded this approach importantly to include additional recursive reflections on the context in which suffering is occurring: a “learning cycle for liberative pastoral praxis.”6 What is different about the method developed in this book is the focus on unconscious process—both intrapsychic and intersubjective—grounded in a relational psychoanalytic approach that neither ignores wider contextual, political, and socioeconomic issues, nor abandons the importance of attention to unconscious dynamics in both partners in the helping relationship and, importantly, shared in the unconscious relationship between them. This method is illustrated in five fictional case studies.

			Chapter 6 describes unconscious motivations behind caregivers’ temptations to ignore standards of practice, including the desire to be a “special carer,” based on empirical research.

			The final chapter offers the reader a relational theology that attempts to bridge new understandings of the multiplicity of the human psyche and the power of unconscious relationship with our understanding of the relationality of the divine. The chapter suggests implications of this theology of multiplicity for pastoral praxis.





What Is New to This Edition


			Two important advances in the field of pastoral theology have been incorporated into the current volume. First, there has been continually increasing attention to concerns of the LGBTQ+ community regarding both sexual orientation and gender identity,7 including the emergence of the voices of trans/nonbinary persons. Second, there has been growing acknowledgment by spiritual and pastoral caregivers of providing culturally and spiritually sensitive care amid increasing religious pluralism in the United States, including those claiming no religion—the “nones” and those who identify as spiritual but not religious—the fastest growing category in the Pew Research Center’s tracking of religious affiliation in the United States between 2007 and 2022.8 Teachers, students, and practitioners of care have recognized the need to greatly increase sensitivity to both intercultural and interreligious engagement, and attentiveness to power dynamics and differentials based on the social location of each person in the caring relationship.

			Greater attention to LGBTQ+ issues and to interreligious engagement is not only reflected in this volume’s new case study of “Chris” and “Malik” but also resulted in small but significant changes throughout. For instance, varying the language for the divine to recognize that not all religious traditions use the same words for “God,” and many religions such as Buddhism and Hinduism are nontheistic (not worshipping a deity) or non-monotheistic/pantheistic (having reverence for multiple deities). I have replaced binary gender phrases such as “he or she” with nonbinary language. In alignment with current editorial practices, I have occasionally used the pronouns “they” and “them” for single subjects, and in every reference to the trans/nonbinary chaplain “Chris.” This may be a challenge for some readers, but I encourage you to carefully work your way through this narrative if the use of plural pronouns for an individual who identifies as nonbinary is unfamiliar to you. Respect for individuals’ choices of gender pronouns is a significant advance in expressions of respect for LGBTQ+ persons.

			One of the most debated matters regarding religious and spiritual pluralism in recent decades has been the issue of terminology for what spiritually-informed caregivers, chaplains, counselors, and therapists actually do. Initially led by hospital, prison, and military chaplains who experienced firsthand the growing heterogeneity of beliefs and affiliations among those they were called to serve, the term spiritual care increasingly has come to replace the term pastoral care in institutional settings where chaplains work.

			The term “pastoral care” was introduced by the mid-twentieth-century founders of the field of pastoral theology and care,9 who were almost entirely Christian clergy and theologians.10 “Pastor” of course means “shepherd,” and the image of pastoral care as “shepherding” (by congregational clergy as well as chaplains and pastoral counselors) was adopted from the image of Jesus in the Gospel of John as the Good Shepherd who even “lays down his life for the sheep” (John 10:11). The term “pastoral” itself was in common usage in Protestant, Catholic, and Orthodox expressions of Christianity going back centuries (e.g., the Cura Pastoralis written c. 590 CE by Pope Gregory I). Feminist pastoral theologians in the 1980s and 1990s, led by Jeanne Stevenson-Moessner,11 critiqued the boundary-blurring sacrificial nature of this Johannine image without rejecting the term “pastoral” outright.

			Expanding numbers of Jewish, Buddhist, and other non-Christian care providers by the turn of the twenty-first century challenged the continuing use of “pastoral” as christocentric and not inclusive of the many ways in which care could be conceived theologically. “Spiritual care” is also more congruent with the rise around the millennium of individuals who adopted the term “spiritual but not religious.” These are persons who either rejected institutional religion due to experiences of harm, or never were raised religiously, but at the same time believed (as many still do) in some form of transcendent power in the universe that could be perceived as bestowing wisdom, growth, and healing. Many atheists and agnostics also have chafed at the term “pastoral” as still carrying more than a whiff of Christianity. Prompted by these multifaceted movements, “spiritual care” has come into common usage both in the United States and internationally by the 2000s as a more inclusive term.12

			The terms “spiritual,” “spirituality,” and “spiritual care” are not without their own problems, however. “Spirituality” and related terms have been critiqued as being so vague and broad that they might mean whatever anyone thinks they mean.13 While “spiritual care” has become the majority usage in secular institutions serving people from a vast number of religious traditions or none, such as hospitals, prisons, and the military, the term “pastoral care” is still often used by Christian practitioners—and based on earlier adoption of the term by Jewish chaplains, is still used by Jewish writers and practitioners,14 and most recently, by some Muslim chaplains as well.15

			To add further complexity to the picture of religious diversity in the United States, theologians are now exploring the phenomenon of multiple religious belonging and arguing for its validity16—with corresponding implications for spiritual care.17 Spiritual caregivers are now encouraged to listen for and validate such spiritual multiplicity rather than rejecting it as “bad theology” or self-contradictory syncretism. Many persons of color, in particular, are embracing indigenous religious practices and beliefs without rejecting the religion in which they were raised. The theory of multiplicity of selves within one “self,” which occupied me in two subsequent books following Shared Wisdom, as well as the idea of multiple religious belonging are included in the current text and further undergird my proposals about the multiplicity of the divine in the final chapter.18

			I have chosen in this volume generally to follow a consensus arrived at during the first conference of the International Association for Spiritual Care. The conference participants (scholars and practitioners not only in the field of pastoral theology but also psychology, medicine, and nursing) shared an understanding that “spiritual care” is an umbrella term for care provided by all well-trained practitioners who can offer care to persons of multiple religious backgrounds (or none) with sensitivity and respect, utilizing sound methods of interreligious engagement.

			“Spiritual” in this context was defined by Kristin Merle as:

			the dynamic dimension of human life that relates to the way persons (individual and community) experience, express and/or seek meaning, purpose and transcendence, and the way they connect to the moment, to self, to others, to the significant and/or the sacred. The spiritual field is multidimensional:

			 				Existential challenges (e.g., questions concerning identity, meaning, suffering and death, guilt and shame, reconciliation and forgiveness, freedom and responsibility, hope and despair, love and joy.

				Value based considerations and attitudes (what is most important for each person, such as relations to oneself, family, friends, work, things, nature, art and culture, ethics and morals, and life itself).

				Religious considerations and foundations (faith, beliefs and practices, the relationship with God or the ultimate).19



			We further understood “pastoral care” to be a tradition-specific form of spiritual care practiced mainly by Christian caregivers, as well as some Jewish and Muslim chaplains, and some from other religious traditions.20

			What this means in practical terms is that a spiritual caregiver should be able to address the immediate existential concerns of all care recipients as a core competency. Spiritual caregivers are (and in my view should be) deeply educated and grounded in their own traditions, but should not proselytize or impose their own religious beliefs on those for whom they are caring. “Pastoral caregivers” are still needed to provide specific rituals and discuss tradition-specific matters of theology with care recipients adhering to Christianity or other particular religions who embrace the term “pastoral.” I will continue to use the term “pastoral care” in this book, not entirely interchangeably with “spiritual care” but also not rejecting it, because as a Christian pastoral theologian, the Johannine imagery is still a part of my tradition—while as a Christian feminist theologian I also agree with the limitations and dangers of the “shepherd” paradigm, and do not see “shepherding” as the only model for Christian practices of care and counseling.

			Pastoral counseling and psychotherapy began as a specialization of ordained clergy and vowed members of religious orders who were counselors or therapists prepared with in-depth religious education (normatively a Master of Divinity) plus additional clinical and theological training. Credentialing was formerly conducted by church-related associations—primarily the American Association of Pastoral Counselors (AAPC)—who required rigorous clinical training, individual and group supervision, adherence to an internal code of ethics, and endorsement by a recognized religious body. Such counselors were certified by examining boards, and could rise with additional training and experience to supervisory status. (This process was modeled after the requirements for certification by psychoanalytic institutes.)

			The term “pastoral counseling” has not been replaced so much as eclipsed by external institutional, legal, and regulatory demands. In the 1980s, state legislation began to be enacted to expand mental health licenses to cover professions other than psychiatrists, psychologists, and clinical social workers to the counseling professions (including professional counselors, professional clinical counselors, mental health counselors, marriage and family therapists, school and vocational counselors) in order to standardize training and normative practices and to provide channels of ethical accountability. As the prevalence of sexual misconduct by therapists was increasingly recognized and malpractice claims increased, the professions themselves saw a need for regulation. States turned to the National Board of Certified Counselors (NBCC) and the American Association for Marriage and Family Therapists (AAMFT) to formulate licensure requirements, including documentation of supervised experience and uniform qualifying exams. Because many state laws were title protection acts—that is, protecting the standards for calling oneself a “counselor,” most pastoral counselors and pastoral counseling trainees entered master’s level degree programs that would qualify them for licensure—and were encouraged by AAPC to do so. It was no longer possible to claim ordination or church employment as a shield against liability for poor practice.

			Today there are still practitioners who identify as pastoral counselors, but their numbers are dwindling, and pastoral counseling training centers, once accredited by AAPC, have mostly dissolved or become internship sites for counseling students with an interest in spiritually-sensitive psychotherapy.21 AAPC was absorbed in 2019 into the Association for Clinical Pastoral Education (the primary chaplaincy training association),22 with former certified AAPC Fellows and Diplomates granted a status within ACPE as “pastoral psychotherapists.”23 ACPE now offers a Spiritually Integrated Psychotherapy (SIP) program for “licensed and pre-licensed mental health professionals . . . who seek to explore the ways spirituality, religion, and the search for meaning influence their own lives and the lives of their clients.”24

			The term “pastoral” also represents an intentional and historical integration of theology and clinical theory and practice that has not been fully replicated by more secular psychotherapies. Many secular helping professionals have increasingly become open to clients’ spirituality as a dimension of their growth and healing—and not merely a neurotic symptom (following Freud!25). The American Psychiatric Association (the publisher of the Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders) has published material intended to provide authoritative guidance for therapists wanting to address clients’ spiritual concerns, and psychologist Kenneth Pargament has contributed significantly to this literature.26

			Even so, few contemporary therapists who genuinely wish to be sensitive to clients’ expressions of religious and spiritual beliefs have the kind of thorough-­going graduate level education in religious beliefs, practices, and theological developments and divisions across centuries that are comparable to the training of pastoral counselors in the twentieth century. The writings of Carrie Doehring, who is both a pastoral theologian and a psychologist, represent a bridge between pastoral theology and the integration of spirituality in secular care and psychotherapy.27 The case study of “Sara” in Shared Wisdom is an example of a pastoral psychotherapist who is well grounded in her own religious tradition and that of her patient.

			Last but by no means least, issues of racism and sexism, already addressed in the first edition, have only become more exigent in the pastoral theology literature in the past decade with the rise of the Black Lives Matter movement—further galvanized by the murders of George Floyd and Breonna Taylor and numerous other African Americans by police and “stand your ground” anti-Black vigilantes—and the rise of white supremacy and masculinism especially in the Christian nationalism movement.28 There has been a powerful right-wing conservative backlash against movements for racial and gender equality. Pastoral and spiritual caregivers and counselors therefore need to be continually alert to the impact of intergenerational and racial trauma, gender-based violence, and other forms of oppression and hate-motivated violence. This makes countertransference all the more important as a matter for attention, since all forms of racial and gender bias are deeply embedded layers of every caregiver’s unconscious—further highlighting the importance of self-examination and the effort to use one’s own experiences as an instrument for empathy and understanding.

			There are additionally a number of smaller changes throughout this volume that reflect the feedback of readers, colleagues, and students; my own continuing engagement with psychoanalytic literature, communities, and history; and the evolving insights of the field of pastoral theology—all of which have deepened my own understanding of the meaning, purpose, and ethical praxis of spiritual care and psychotherapy over many years.





How to Use This Book


			There are many ways to use this book. You may choose to read it from cover to cover. This will give you the most in-depth historical, theoretical, and theological grounding as well as an overview of how countertransference and the use of the self may be applied specifically in the different disciplines of congregation-based pastoral care and brief counseling, chaplaincy, and pastoral psychotherapy. You may, however, prefer to turn first to a chapter that most immediately addresses your particular pastoral focus or profession and then read other chapters for the background you find most relevant to your work. Especially if you are involved in continuing education, a seminary class, or training workshop, you may also choose to follow one or more of the vignettes in the introduction, and through chapters 3, 4, and 5, one at a time, to stimulate focused case study discussion.29 These chapters are intended for practitioners at all levels of training and experience, since the need for self-reflection follows us throughout our professional lives.

			Finally, every reader is encouraged to read the final chapter, which is a theological/spiritual reflection on a more multiple and relational understanding of God and on deepening our own contemplative practices of prayer or meditation. We cannot access our awareness of our self-experience and the life of feelings unless we also nurture our selves emotionally, physically, intellectually, and spiritually. As we nurture our desire for the divine/the sacred/the transcendent, we are replenishing a wellspring for the love of both self and neighbor that is at the heart of this book.





Acknowledgments


			 			I am grateful for all those who have used the first edition of this book as a textbook, and those who have read it as students and pastoral care and counseling colleagues over the past two decades. When invited over the years to present ideas from the book to pastoral, chaplaincy, and psychotherapist organizations, I learned so much more than I already thought I “knew” in my imagination about the characters represented in the original four case studies. I am grateful to those colleagues who studied the case stories with great care, and vulnerably shared from their own experience in reflecting on the material. Over the years, I have also solicited feedback from readers and tucked away many thoughtful comments that have now enriched this new edition. Any errors or omissions in this volume are entirely my own.

			Heartfelt appreciation goes to those who offered their time and expertise to give detailed feedback on portions of this revised edition. I’m very grateful for my editor for this volume at Fortress Press, Yvonne D. Hawkins. Her enthusiasm and careful shepherding of this second edition have been invaluable! Special thanks go to Muaz Chaudhry, Arifa Khandwala, Afsheen A. Shamsi, and Aleksandr Soran for their thoughtful feedback on the new case study of “Chris.”

			I am indebted to the faculty in the Interreligious Engagement field at Union Theological Seminary, New York, where I learned so much from my colleagues over eight years, especially Jerusha Rhodes, for her encouragement and for educating all of us on faculty regarding ethical interreligious engagement. The collective witness for social justice of all my Union faculty colleagues and students have been formational for me, and Union continues to be an important community of accountability for me as I have envisioned this second edition. Special thanks also go to Rabbi Mychal Springer, formerly of Jewish Theological Seminary and now the Clinical Pastoral Education (CPE) Manager at New York-Presbyterian Hospital. She has been an important conversation partner and friend (and co-­instructor!) who has also contributed to my growth in interfaith dialogue and teaching. I remain very grateful for the generosity and wisdom of the many colleagues who read drafts of the first edition published in 2004 and whose names are listed there.

			Deep thanks are also due to the Episcopal Church Foundation for providing me with a three-year fellowship to pursue the research summarized in chapter 6 and for their ongoing support of the Fellows’ Forum. I am also grateful for the intellectual stimulation and support during the writing of the first edition from my many colleagues and students at the Lutheran Theological Seminary at Philadelphia (now United Lutheran Seminary in Philadelphia and Gettysburg, Pennsylvania) over the nine years I spent on that faculty. That rich, multicultural, and warmly collegial seminary community provided an exceptionally fertile ground in which this project could grow!

			My work has been continually informed by the support and academic rigor of my colleagues in the Psychology, Culture, and Religion Group (PCR)1 of the American Academy of Religion, the Society for Pastoral Theology,2 and the International Association for Spiritual Care (IASC).3 I am also greatly indebted to the Sigmund Freud Museum in Vienna, where I was privileged to do research as the 2013–2014 Fulbright-Freud Scholar of Psychoanalysis and I continue to learn from colleagues and programs associated with the museum. I have also learned much from ongoing contacts with psychoanalytic and therapist colleagues, especially those affiliated with the International Association of Relational Psychoanalysis and Psychotherapy. Lisa Cataldo, Mary Ragan, and Melanie Suchet have been especially important conversation partners.

			Warm thanks also go to my clergy and lay colleagues in several Episcopal congregations where I have served as assisting priest over the years, including St. Martin-in-the-Fields Episcopal Church in Philadelphia; Holy Trinity Church in Decatur, Georgia; and the Cathedral of St. John the Divine in New York City. These communities have been the spiritual holding environments for my continued vocational discernment as a teacher, pastor, and writer, with special recognition to my dear friend and priest colleague the Reverend Sharline Fulton in Philadelphia, and spiritual directors Cathy Looker SSJ, Phillip Bennett, and Jane Spickett.

			Thanks also to my daughter Macrina Dieffenbach, PhD, whose research expertise has greatly sharpened my own attention to detail and the importance of scientific inquiry! Most of all, I am grateful to my spouse, Michael Cooper-White, whose thoughtful and detailed reading of multiple versions of the book has been invaluable. I am grateful beyond words for his patient listening, insightful questions, and always loving encouragement! Over the years we have become each other’s go-to reader, editor, and sometime writing partner, which has deeply enriched our life’s journey together.





Introduction


			 			Linda


			Linda (she/her) felt light-headed as she sat in the church council meeting, attempting to project an attitude of calm that she did not feel. In her first year as pastor, Linda had introduced a practice of opening every church council meeting with check-ins during which members shared something important about what was going on in their lives. Following the check-ins, the council prayed for each member and for the congregation and the community. At this meeting, Yvonne, the oldest member of the council and a respected elder of the church, had quietly announced that she had been diagnosed with a recurrence of breast cancer and that this time it had metastasized to her liver. She had been given six months to a year to live. She planned to resign from the council after this meeting but had wanted to come and deliver the news in person. She seemed wan and tired but wanted to stay for the meeting and just be with the group for a while.

			The council members at first received this news with shocked silence. Yvonne had seemed to be doing so well, and her cancer had been in remission so long that no one thought of her anymore as a “cancer victim,” but rather as a “cancer survivor.” During prayer, a few members began to weep. The sense of dignity and self-containment Yvonne projected seemed to preclude hugging, but Linda did reach out and quickly squeeze Yvonne’s hand. The group slowly found its way toward the evening’s agenda. Although Yvonne herself was interested in “getting down to business,” Linda felt tearful and distracted from the work at hand. As her eyes wandered to the old grandfather clock in the corner of the meeting room, she kept thinking of her grandmother Eleanor, who had died of cancer a few years before. Linda’s mind filled with thoughts of how much she missed her, and how many times in recent years she had wished her grandmother could have been with her, to witness her graduation from seminary, her ordination, and the birth of her first child.





Gary


			Gary (he/him) threw on his blue chaplain’s coat and looked over the call list for the morning. He was relieved that this was a day off for his CPE students, so he could simply do pastoral visits and quietly catch up on work at his desk. Two volatile, angry students, Amanda and Jake, had gotten into it with each other in group supervision the day before, and after twelve years of supervising, Gary was feeling more than a little burned out on facilitating intense group processes. Adding to this, he is experiencing a persistent low-level depression and irritability following a recent breakup with his girlfriend, Susan, whom he had imagined marrying—someday—and starting a family. Susan had been increasingly exasperated by Gary’s reluctance to make a firm commitment. After making a “fish or cut bait” ultimatum, Susan had tearfully broken off their relationship.

			Gary took the elevator to the seventh floor, the pediatric ward, and knocked on the door of room 716, where an eight-year-old boy named Daniel had just been moved from intensive care. Gary had learned from the nurses that Daniel was recovering from injuries sustained in a severe car accident, in which his mother’s new boyfriend, Stan, had been driving drunk. Daniel’s mother was also in the hospital, recovering from chest and facial injuries. Stan had been discharged after brief treatment in the ER and had not been heard from since. Daniel’s father had been contacted where he lived out of state but had declined to come see him.

			In the room, Gary found Daniel propped up in the hospital bed, surrounded by Nintendo game cards and one well-loved, scruffy stuffed elephant, a relic from earlier childhood. Daniel was watching cartoons on TV. Gary felt his heart lurch as he saw how thin and pale Daniel looked under the light hospital blanket. In spite of years of experience, Gary always felt awkward talking with children. Nevertheless, he somehow managed to initiate a connection with Daniel that seemed to open the door for further conversation about his loneliness, his feelings of abandonment by both his parents, and how God often felt very far away, too. Gary left the room after promising to return the next day, thinking how he might make contact with Daniel’s parents.

			As he headed for the elevator, Cora, the hospital social worker, approached him and said, “Daniel will be ready for discharge soon, but his mother won’t, and there is no one to look after him. His grandmother isn’t well and is already overburdened by taking in Daniel’s younger sibs. Daniel will still need some ongoing outpatient medical care. I was wondering, do you know any church people who are already qualified as foster parents who could take care of Daniel for just a week or two?” Gary was surprised at how much he wanted to blurt out, “Daniel can come stay at my house! It’s just me and my two cats, and there’s plenty of room!” He bit his tongue and simply said, “Hmm, what a good idea. Let me think about that.” But Daniel’s face haunted him as he continued to make his rounds the rest of the day.





Terence


			Terence (he/him) stormed out of the rectory, got into his Lexus, and slammed the car door, shutting out the last angry words his wife, Jane, was shouting at him from the front steps. “Dammit,” he thought, “she knows how much pressure I’m already under. She knows I’m on the short list for bishop of Central Florida, and God only knows, I’m the right man for the job! And now she has to make a mountain out of a molehill about Susan—on whom I have not laid a finger, not once!” His tires screeched as he turned out of the driveway and drove the mile and a half to St. George’s, the large, wealthy white suburban church he had served as rector for three years.

			The occasion for his wife’s angry accusations was this morning’s appointment: a meeting with Susan, the church treasurer. He was irritated that his day had already started badly. He admitted to himself that he looked forward to seeing Susan, whose shy smiles and light touches on his arm had become restorative for him these last few months. Susan’s cool, light manner had been a welcome relief from the demands of home, with three teenage children and a wife who seemed perpetually frazzled and wanting more of his time. Susan never seemed to demand anything, and her efficient work with the church’s financial accounts had in fact lightened his load considerably.

			When Terence arrived at his office, his secretary, Betty, informed him in her usual gruff, matter-of-fact tone that she had had to squeeze in a pastoral appointment first thing that morning. Terence inwardly groaned but then brightened as Betty told him, “I thought you’d want to see this one right away. It’s Elaine Bartles, and she wants to talk to you again about some marital issues.” At that moment, Elaine came into the office, leaning slightly on a walking cane, and said, “I’m sorry to take up your time when you’re so busy, but I really do need to talk to you, and your sermon on Sunday was just so inspiring to me about taking hold and changing what’s wrong in my life.” “No problem at all,” Terence murmured in a soothing voice. Elaine’s eyes welled up with tears as Terence ushered her into his office, his hand lightly touching her back. As his office door closed, Betty caught the scent of lilac perfume and pursed her mouth in what was becoming a habitual feeling of cynicism and dislike toward “our glad-handing priest.”





Chris


			Chris (they/them) was thrilled to be hired straight out of divinity school as the first trans/nonbinary chaplain at Mountbrook College,1 a small liberal arts college in the Midwest. They arrived early in July and were eager to begin implementing their vision for a more religiously diverse chaplaincy program. Early one Saturday morning in October, Chris was surprised to receive a text message from the provost: “Please come to my office at 9 a.m. today. Urgent!” Chris was still getting their footing at the college, but wondered what could be so urgent at the sleepiest time of the week on the small college campus. Feeling a sense of dread, they hastily dressed and jogged the half-mile across campus to the Admin Building. Most of the offices were closed, but as Chris opened the door to the president’s suite, they saw the provost, Alice Johnson, standing alongside a Pakistani student, Malik. Malik was slightly familiar to Chris from the Muslim student gathering during fall orientation. Malik now had bandages on his forehead and cheek, and ugly bruises along both arms. “Please, have a seat,” Alice said, and Chris perched nervously on a bench while Malik collapsed into an armchair. Alice remained standing and looked grim. “Chris, I think you’ve met Malik—he’s a freshman here? Malik, this is Chaplain Chris.” Both nodded.

			“I know it’s early on a Saturday, but we have a situation we have to deal with immediately, and we wanted to give you both a heads up before we send out a statement,” she said. “Last night after the football game, Malik was physically assaulted by a group of three sophomores—two male and one female, all white—over behind the bleachers. One of Malik’s friends, Desmond, had started to leave the game, thinking Malik was right behind him, but then he heard a commotion and turned back. He saw the whole incident and heard the students shouting racial and anti-Muslim slurs at Malik. Desmond called campus police, who got there quickly since they had been at the game. They took Malik to the local ER, where he was treated, and Desmond drove him back to their dorm. The students responsible for the attack are now at the local police station waiting for their parents to arrive, and to learn what charges they will be facing.”

			Alice paused for the story to sink in, then said, “I thought you should meet Malik, so you could provide him with some support. Since he’s only been at the college about a month, he doesn’t know many Muslims here (well, as you know, we don’t have many Muslim students), and he wasn’t at the hospital long enough to receive chaplain services. I also called you down here early so that as chaplain you would be prepared for whatever responses we may receive after we release a short announcement to the campus community.”

			Malik looked from the provost to the chaplain and his eyes became wide with anxiety. Chris said to Alice, “I’d like to meet with Malik one-on-one just for a bit. I’d like to hear from him about what kinds of support I might be able to provide.” Turning to Malik, Chris asked, “Would you like to come over to the chapel office and talk with me?” Malik nodded and said, very formally, “Thank you. I will come.” Chris thanked Alice for calling them, and said, “Let me know how I can help as things go forward.”





Sara


			Sara (she/ella) practiced pastoral psychotherapy out of a pleasant, sunny parish office that had been lovingly decorated by members of her local church community. Together, they had furnished the office with beautiful plants, a favorite watercolor painting, matching rocking chairs for her and her clients, and a basket of teddy bears and rag dolls. Her last appointment this afternoon was with Miranda, a thirty-one-year-old bank officer with a history of migraine headaches and panic attacks. Sara contemplated Miranda’s name in her calendar with a mixture of affection and worry. Miranda had been seeing Sara for over two years and, after a long while, had confided several stories of sexual abuse at the hands of her uncle and one older brother.

			Miranda recently had begun speaking to Sara in a small, baby voice, sitting on the floor close to Sara’s knees, and clutching one of the teddy bears to her chest. Miranda would sometimes curl up there on the floor and sob with wordless grief and terror, and Sara had, just once during Miranda’s last session, reached down and touched Sara’s hair in a gesture of nurture and support. Miranda had looked up and smiled through her tears, and suddenly grabbed Sara’s knees and hugged her legs. They had stayed in that position until the session ended ten minutes later, when without prompting Miranda slowly stood up, resumed her usual rather stiff adult posture, and wordlessly left the office. Miranda was now ten minutes late. Sara began to pace the office, wondering if the memories Miranda had accessed in the last session were overwhelming her. She began to consider that perhaps she should call Miranda to offer her an appointment at her own house, where Miranda might feel even more nurtured and safe.





Case Studies at Crossroads


			These five vignettes have something in common. The clergy professionals in these case studies are at a critical crossroads in their ministries.2 Linda, Gary, Terence, Chris, and Sara, all in their own way, are faced with challenges that seem to be from an external source but actually are more internal and rooted deeply in each one’s intimate history.

			Linda is at risk for drifting off into a preoccupation with unresolved grief over a beloved grandmother’s death. This preoccupation is distracting her from tasks that need her attention and, perhaps more perilously, will draw her into an over-close relationship of needy nurturing of Yvonne, which Yvonne has not requested.

			Gary is lonely and experiencing some symptoms of burnout in certain areas of his work. He feels awkward around children but able to make an unusually strong connection with the child Daniel. His heart goes out to Daniel, and he finds himself attracted to the role of the better parent for this child, who strikes him as a fragile, sensitive waif.

			Terence is feeling the burdens and pressures of his own success, defined in terms of a ladder of achievement that he has been climbing rapidly his entire life. He is preoccupied with living up to a self-image of importance and accomplishment, and often feels thwarted in his ambitions by the time demands of his wife and children. He enjoys the attentions of women in the parish who alternately idealize, soothe, and stimulate him in ways he does not experience at home. He is at risk for crossing a boundary, perhaps sexual, perhaps simply inappropriately intimate, as his parishioners begin to serve his emotional needs on his meteoric climb toward being a bishop.

			Chris is facing their first campus crisis. They are quickly discovering that the skills they had learned in their hospital internship, supported by constant supervision, seem less clear-cut now that they are the sole chaplain to an entire college. Chris feels confident in their commitments to anti-racism and interreligious diversity, but they identify as a Christian/Humanist, and have never actually ministered to a Muslim patient during their training. Chris’s instinct was good to arrange to hear more from Malik directly—although inviting him to an isolated office in a chapel still furnished with numerous Christian symbols was certainly problematic. To claim their sense of authority to cover their insecurity, and to appear professional in the eyes of the administration, they are at risk for trying to take charge in ways that could prove unempathic, even harmful to Malik.

			Sara had already found herself at least two paces down a hazardous fork in the road when her patient3 Miranda fell out of words into a profoundly emotional enactment and Sara stayed with her in a long moment of silent physical closeness. Sara now is at risk for continuing down that path of nonverbal care and nurture, because she finds herself so deeply moved by Miranda’s vulnerability and trust, and also is enjoying how good it feels to be so needed.

			These five clergy professionals are at crossroads but not necessarily at precipices. There is no need to assume that any of these five will automatically fall into dangerous or unconsidered enactments with the individuals in their care. In each case, there is an alternative path, which because of the very heightened degree of personal feeling and potential for affective involvement, could be a particularly powerful moment of healing and grace for those in their care. The key to each of these clergy professionals’ choices and actions is the subject of this book: countertransference, or the use of the self in pastoral care and counseling.

			For the purpose of this book, the term “countertransference” will refer to the sum total of thoughts, feelings, fantasies, impulses, and bodily sensations—conscious and unconscious—that may arise in the pastoral caregiver in relation to any person who has come for help. This represents a departure—or more accurately, a further development—from the classical psychoanalytic definition of countertransference, in which many generations of pastoral caregivers were trained. Based on the work of Sigmund Freud,4 the classical definition of countertransference refers to unconscious distortions in the helper’s perception due to unresolved internal issues usually rooted in early childhood. The concept originated as the counterpart to the “transference,” Freud’s term for the patient’s projections, or transferring of experiences of parents and other powerful figures from childhood onto the therapist (or helping professional or authority figure). From a classical point of view, the work of a caregiver around countertransference is to analyze and thereby neutralize any neurotic “baggage” that might hinder the work of therapy or care.

			A newer view,5 sometimes termed “totalist,”6 encompasses this classical understanding but also expands beyond it. In this new understanding, which I prefer to call “intersubjective countertransference,”7 countertransference still includes preconditioned patterns of relating that are developed in the helping professional’s own childhood, but now is understood to be strongly influenced or even evoked by the transference of the helpee8 (also referred to by psychoanalyst Donna Orange as the therapist’s “cotransference”9). This is not a new idea in psychoanalytic theory (especially in “object relations” theory), but its importance is increasingly being noted among relational psychoanalysts, and the evolution of this understanding of countertransference will be discussed in detail in the next chapter. Transference, similarly then, is defined as the sum total of the helpee’s thoughts, feelings, fantasies, impulses, and bodily sensations—conscious and unconscious—toward the helping professional, and may include, but again is not limited to, preconditioned patterns of relating developed in childhood.

			In this understanding, transference and countertransference operate together to form a complex interrelation, now understood by a number of analysts and theorists as “intersubjectivity,” beginning with self psychologists also steeped in philosophy in the mid-1990s.10 Intersubjectivity operates at both conscious and unconscious levels. “Self” and “other” become intertwined in this relational matrix. Aspects of both “selfness” and “otherness” may be discovered in numerous places: internally in one’s own thoughts, fantasies, and memories; externally in relation to the actual other; and in the “between” of the relationship itself—the interstices of the relationship as it is continually being co-constructed. This complex relation informs and generates patterns of meaning-making, which may be enacted nonverbally, and also affects the quality of the healing (positive or negative) that evolves in the helping relationship over time. Countertransference is therefore not only an obstacle to understanding the patient, but becomes a valuable tool for understanding the other.

			It is still no less important to attend to one’s own neurotic vulnerabilities (from which no one is immune). Self-examination still needs to include awareness of our own “buttons” or tender places within ourselves that are vulnerable to being pushed by others. This classical contribution to the understanding of countertransference has been fruitfully taught in CPE and in pastoral care curricula for generations. However, the argument of the present book is that countertransference is no longer just a hindrance to be worked through or analyzed away. As the helper’s total response, both conscious and unconscious toward the helpee, countertransference is also a valuable instrument for listening to the other, because in the unconscious relationship that grows between helper and helpee, our own thoughts, feelings, and fantasies literally absorb the thought life of the other, as we share in the intimacy of the helpee’s shared confidences. To put it most simply, our understanding of the power of this intersubjective countertransference should prompt our reflection on the question: What might my own feelings and reactions be telling me about my helpee’s feelings? By listening for the music of our own inner world as we sit with another, we listen to the other’s music as well, through the counterpoint (both harmonious and dissonant) of shared wisdom that springs up between us. This is how the use of the self becomes a treasure for helping another.

			My interest in the topic of countertransference and the use of the self, like all good research topics, was “overdetermined” by both personal and clinical experience as a pastoral psychotherapist. My psychoanalytically informed clinical training began when this intersubjective understanding of countertransference was becoming an increasingly strong focus of attention in the field of psychotherapy. This was a strong subjective fit for my personal history, which caused me to develop a high degree of interpersonal sensitivity and attunement—at least in certain situations. These factors, combined with my subsequent clinical experience, particularly with survivors of severe trauma and abuse, have resulted in a style of therapeutic practice that relies heavily on my own inner felt experiences of the patient from moment to moment, across all levels of awareness: cognitive, affective, and somatic.

			I do, of course, sift these experiences for aspects that relate more to my own subjectivity and biases. But I regard them, and have come increasingly to trust them, as an important and reliable channel of information about the inner world of the patient. I often feel as if I have “caught” something from this other person from across the room. I have come to trust that the more vivid this experience is in me, the more unmetabolized the experience is likely to be in the patient’s own psyche, and therefore the more in need of being held—initially in my own awareness, until it can be handed back to the patient in words.

			This in turn initiates an important therapeutic sequence: (1) bringing the unmetabolized and unnamed mental contents up to a level of conscious awareness (however inchoate), (2) symbolizing this material via words or images that can be given words, and finally (3) facilitating an integrative process of meaning-making and working through.11 This psychoanalytic approach to healing is further explored in chapter 5 on the use of the self in pastoral psychotherapy.

			My work in the 1990s in the area of abuse prevention and, in particular, the subject of clergy professional boundaries and sexual ethics also heightened my interest in countertransference as it relates to the need for clergy self-­awareness.12 In my doctoral research, I sought to explore further the area of pastoral counselors’ self-experience and self-care within the therapeutic relationship, including from a more psychodynamic perspective. There is already a sizable body of literature on professional sexual ethics,13 and it is not the purpose of this book to duplicate that work. My question was and still is: Why, now after decades of boundary trainings for clergy and other religious teachers and leaders, and the implementation of ethics policies in many denominational bodies, do some religious professionals still feel the rules do not apply to them? And why do some denominational and congregational authorities continue to turn a blind eye and cover up abuse? What unconscious impulses might be at work? I also broadened my research beyond situations where sexual or other ethical boundaries were violated, to explore sexual feelings in the countertransference where there were no apparent inappropriate enactments. Such research, further, explores not only the negative and/or ethically prohibitive aspects of countertransference, but also the possibility for positive and constructive psychodynamic aspects of countertransference as a useful tool in understanding a patient’s reality, whereby the helper’s own self becomes an instrument for attentiveness, and a tool for greater empathy.14 Findings from this research are detailed in chapter 6.

			Readings in object relations and relational theory have further convinced me that it is artificial to attempt to isolate sexual or sexualized aspects of countertransference from aggressive aspects, narcissistic and other characterological structures in the personality, and other unconscious influences that may be at work in the intersubjective field between helper and helpee. I have increasingly come to suspect that isolating the sexual dimension from other aspects of the countertransference may inadvertently collude with the splitting off of sexual feelings from other dimensions of the helper’s self-experience. Paradoxically, this can result in an increased risk for ethical violations via harmful enactments of what is dissociated. As one writer on clergy boundary violations, Carrie Doehring, suggests, it is often the aggressive component and not the sexual that is suppressed from conscious awareness. In her discussion of John Updike’s novel A Month of Sundays, the aggressive component of the offending minister’s countertransference is enacted sexually and then disguised under the rubric of “sex addiction.”15

			Therefore, self-awareness is key, both in preventing ineffective or even unethical interventions and, importantly, in understanding the complex layers of communication, both conscious and unconscious, between helper and helpee. The guiding conviction of this book is that the more thoughtfully these layers of conscious and unconscious communication are explored, especially by the helper through self-examination and consultation, the more effective the pastoral care or counseling will be. The understanding gained by introspection will enable a reduction in impulsive actions and unethical interventions, and create conditions for a much richer, safer, and more genuinely helping relationship. What one actually does with this deeper awareness of self and other will differ, depending on whether the specific relationship is one of congregation-based pastoral care, chaplaincy, or longer-term, psychodynamic pastoral psychotherapy. For example, in congregation-based pastoral care, it would be inadvisable to delve deliberately into unconscious material with a congregation member who has come for help. In long-term pastoral psychotherapy, however, this would likely be the primary focus of therapeutic conversation. In either case, the self-awareness is the same and can be cultivated in a wide range of pastoral professions.

			Our self-understanding will be put to different uses depending on the type of helping we are doing. But the basic concept is the same: the more we are able to tune in to our own multiple and even conflicting inner perceptions and to reflect on these in a thoughtful way, the more sensitively we will also be able to tune in to the nuances of the helpee’s own feelings, wishes, and experiences. We can, in turn, use these insights to choose responses and interventions that are most appropriate to our role as helpers and to the helpee’s needs. This is the use of the self in pastoral care and counseling.





Chapter One


				A History of the Concept of Countertransference


			 			The theory of intersubjectivity and the concept of an unconscious relationship were fully developed only in the latter part of the twentieth century. The notion of a relationship as a truly two-person co-construction of reality, as opposed to two isolated subjects each regarding the other as an object, has been most fully developed in tandem with certain concepts relating to postmodernism, in particular, the concept of social constructivism.

			The idea that one’s thoughts and feelings could penetrate and thereby communicate with the psyche of another is not new, however. During the same time Freud was formulating his concept of countertransference, he also wrote, “It is a very remarkable thing that the Unconscious of one human being can react upon that of another, without passing through the Conscious.”1 From its birth as an idea, countertransference has been understood to some degree as an arena in which the unconscious of the helpee touches the unconscious of the helper in a deep way. This chapter traces the history of the concept with particular attention to the idea of an unconscious relationship and the helper’s use of self in the healing process.





Freud


			Sigmund Freud discussed the countertransference in two published papers.2 In the first paper, “The Future Prospects of Psychoanalytic Therapy,” written in 1910, he introduces the term almost as an aside, but in admonitory tones: “We have become aware of the ‘counter-transference,’ which arises in him [the physician] as a result of the patient’s influence on his unconscious feelings, and we are almost inclined to insist that he shall recognize this counter-transference in himself and overcome it.”3 In 1915 in “Observations on Transference-Love,” he continued in this cautionary mood: “Our control over ourselves is not so complete that we may not suddenly one day go further than we intended. In my opinion, therefore, we ought not to give up the neutrality toward the patient, which we have acquired through keeping the counter-transference in check.”4 In both papers, the tone is one of vigilance against seduction by a patient’s conscious and unconscious erotic pulls on the therapist’s affections. The only safeguard is an ever-attentive maintenance of emotional neutrality. It was during this same period that Freud wrote the following well-known statement likening the attitude of the analyst to the “emotional coldness” of a surgeon:

			I cannot advise my colleagues too urgently to model themselves during psychoanalytic treatment on the surgeon, who puts aside all his feelings, even his human sympathy, and concentrates his mental forces on the single aim of performing the operation as skillfully as possible . . . The justification for requiring this emotional coldness is that it creates the most advantageous conditions for both parties: for the doctor a desirable protection for his own emotional life and for the patient the largest amount of help we can give him to-day. A surgeon of earlier times took as his motto the words: “Je le pensai, Dieu le guerit.” (“I dressed his wounds, God cured him.”) The analyst should be content with something similar.5

			The discovery of countertransference was linked to a crisis, as was the idea of transference itself a decade before. Just as Freud’s discovery of the concept of transference can be traced back to his rigorous scrutiny of his own failed treatment with “Dora” in the late 1890s,6 the discovery of countertransference grew out of Freud’s dismay over a complex and destructive sexual relationship between his Swiss colleague C.G. Jung and Jung’s patient (and eventual doctoral advisee) Sabina Spielrein.7 Freud’s first-known use of the term “countertransference” appears in a letter to Jung, urging the younger man to keep his sexual urges in check. The avuncular and reassuring tone of the letter belies Freud’s fear about the wider implications of the problem for psychoanalysis as a profession, as well as the potential damage to their friendship:

			Such experiences, though painful, are necessary and hard to avoid. Without them we cannot really know life and what we are dealing with. I myself have never been taken in quite so badly, but I have come very close to it a number of times and had a narrow escape. I believe that only grim necessities weighing on my work, and the fact that I was ten years older than yourself when I came to psychoanalysis have saved me from such experiences. But no lasting harm is done. They help us to develop the thick skin we need and to dominate the “countertransference,” which is after all a permanent problem for us; they teach us to displace our own affects to best advantage. They are a blessing in disguise. The way these women manage to charm us with every conceivable psychic perfection until they have attained their purpose is one of nature’s greatest spectacles.8

			Freud saw countertransference as a threat, not only to individuals in treatment, but also to the reputation of psychoanalysis as a profession. His ensuing public silence on the topic reflected a growing sense of alarm. Freud wrote privately to Jung shortly before the breakup of their friendship, “I believe an article on ‘countertransference’ is sorely needed; of course we could not publish it, we should have to circulate it among ourselves.”9 In a thinly veiled reference to Jung, Freud also wrote this in his paper on “transference-love”:

			For the doctor, ethical motives unite with technical ones to restrain him from giving the patient his love. Younger men especially, who are not yet bound by a permanent tie may succumb to the danger of forgetting the rules of technique and the physician’s task for the sake of a wonderful experience . . . And yet it is quite out of the question for the analyst to give way. However highly he may prize love he must prize even more highly the opportunity for helping his patient over a decisive stage in her life.10

			Freud’s view of the transference itself was one of a pitched battle against the unconscious forces in the patient that threaten both patient and analyst. Freud (without yet recognizing the role of his own countertransference) concluded that Dora’s therapy had failed because he had not seen Dora’s erotic transference to him.11 In the decade that followed, Freud gained a great deal from clinical experience. In the case of the “Rat Man,” Freud actually prevented this patient from abruptly quitting therapy as Dora had done, by naming and interpreting his hostile transference.12

			Freud’s classic definition of transference, stated most clearly in “The Dynamics of the Transference,”13 can be summarized as follows: An individual’s patterns of relating develop early in childhood, particularly patterns of loving (including conditions for loving, impulses, gratifications, and aims). These become habitual and reproduce themselves in other relationships throughout life. Some of these patterns are conscious, and some undergo modifications later in life. However, deep instinctual impulses (that is, those having to do with the twin “drives” of sex and aggression) may get held up in development and go underground, where they can be expressed only in fantasy or buried in the unconscious. These unconscious impulses, Freud said, are most likely to be aroused in persons whose need for love is not being satisfactorily gratified in reality in their external life.

			Transference revives and re-enacts primal experiences of parental figures, as well as with siblings and other important influences on the child’s development—especially feelings of helplessness and the anxieties and defenses arising from the utter dependency situation of earliest childhood. Transference manifests itself in extreme reactions or distortions in the patient’s perception of the analyst (or another helping figure). Any person in one’s adult life who is in a role of authority can evoke the same unconscious feelings of dependence, anxiety, and psychic defense—a health professional, a teacher, a boss, a religious leader (with the heightened spiritual status that may tap the most existential layer of nonverbal memory and affect). This is how transference may be recognized for what it is in the analytic situation: it is characterized in particular by its excess, in both character and degree, over what is rational and justifiable. The unconscious erotic component of the transference and the hostile or aggressive component would also manifest themselves as the arena in which the patient would exhibit resistance to the interpretive process. This resistance was what Freud would then analyze, eventually helping patients to understand the unconscious origins of the intensity of their feelings toward the person of the analyst, thereby reducing the likelihood of similar irrational and potentially destructive distortions occurring in other relationships in the patient’s life.

			Freud concluded “The Dynamics of the Transference” in the language of a heroic military struggle. A struggle must be engaged to draw the relationship back to reality. The conflict occurs between three pairs of opposites: doctor versus patient, intellect versus instinct, and recognition (insight) versus striving for discharge (what in later terminology we might refer to as “acting out”). The transference is the battleground on which “the victory must be won.”14 Victory will mean nothing less than recovery from neurosis. The transference can never be forgotten or ignored. Nothing else brings the patient’s inner conflict more directly into the analytic container, and the immediacy of the libido manifesting itself in the here and now moment is what makes the struggle so powerful and so curative. Freud granted the libido a strong measure of autonomy and even will, anthropomorphizing it by investing it with the quality of a worthy adversary. In his last sentence, he states, “It is impossible to destroy anyone in absentia or in effigie.”15 Through transference, the libido shows itself in the here-and-now analytic moment where it can be vanquished.

			The heroic, military tone of this paper may reflect a consciousness of the gathering political tensions in Austria, which erupted in World War I two years later. But the heroic tone also reflects the forces of opposition with which Freud was contending personally and professionally in the years leading up to the war. Freud’s concern whether psychoanalysis elicited transference dynamics more intensely than other therapies, and his references to Jung in this period, have an increasingly defensive quality. In the midst of an atmosphere of growing tension, a potentially ruinous sexual relationship between Jung and his patient (and doctoral advisee) Sabina Spielrein exploded. Spielrein sought out Freud as a consultant to repair the damage caused by what Jung himself later called his “piece of knavery.”16 Spielrein briefly joined Freud’s psychoanalytic society in 1911 after fleeing Jung’s influence in Zürich, and was now a respected doctor publishing innovative psychoanalytic papers herself.17 It was in the very year (1911/12) that Freud was writing “The Dynamics of the Transference” that Spielrein approached him to help her get over the feeling that her life had been “smashed” by Jung.

			Freud’s recommendation to therapists to emulate the surgeon’s “emotional coldness” was, therefore, a reactive one, not a fully rational one. From case reports, this surgical coldness seems to have been a recommendation he himself honored more in the breach. After his break with Jung and his publication of “Observations on Transference Love” in 1915, which was in some sense his effort to put closure on that painful period, Freud never wrote about the subject again.





Ferenczi and His Circle


			The disturbing and personal origins of the discovery of countertransference generated an air of secrecy about the subject similar to the incest taboo itself (which Jung’s sexual explotation of Spielrein had replicated). This infusion of the concept from the beginning with secrecy and shame was perpetuated through the decades like a “phobic dread,” suppressing open discussion about it among analysts,18 and even much introspection on the subject. Analysts in Freud’s close circle did not take up the subject, with two notable exceptions: Sándor Ferenczi and Otto Rank. The eventual ruptures between Freud and Ferenczi and Rank were also due, then, in part, to the taboo surrounding countertransference.

			Ferenczi and Rank are generally credited with being the first to introduce the practice of discussing with the patient the immediate experience of what was happening between them in the room.19 ln addition, Ferenczi’s “active technique” involved close scrutiny of the countertransference, stemming from Ferenczi’s belief that the patient was intuitively aware of the therapist’s real feelings, biases, blind spots, and areas of unanalyzed neurosis. Ferenczi believed that the analyst, far from being a “blank screen,” was an individual whose emotional responses had a direct impact on the analysis, for good or for ill. Ferenczi even advocated occasional disclosure of the analyst’s feelings to the patient.20 This was considered by Freud and most of his followers as a radical departure from accepted technique, and it contributed to the eventual rupture between Freud and Ferenczi in the 1920s.21 Ferenczi also recognized the traumatic impact of sexual abuse, which Freud had earlier recanted.22

			As the founder of his own Hungarian Psycho-analytical Society, Ferenczi was painfully marginalized from the mainstream of Freud’s circle, but this also freed him to develop his own methods. Most influential among Ferenczi’s students were his translator and literary executor, Michael Balint, and Balint’s wife, Alice. A paper the Balints wrote in 1939 anticipates by five decades many ideas that are now current concerning the inevitability of mutual influence between patient and analyst.23 The Balints’ main interest was the treatment of extremely troubled patients, in whom they identified the notion of a “basic fault” arising from very early parental emotional neglect or abuse.24 The intensification of feelings arising in the helper from severely abused and anguished helpees has, in fact, been the major impetus for theoretical investigation of countertransference in every professional generation—going back to Spielrein herself.

			The following statement of the Balints highlights their increasing recognition of the mutuality of feelings in the therapeutic process, which became both controversial and ultimately influential: “The analytical situation is the result of an interplay between the patient’s transference and the analyst’s countertransference, complicated by the reactions released in each other by the other’s transference to him.”25 Beyond these contributions, there was a virtual silence on the subject of countertransference.26 This would change, but not in Freud’s own lifetime.





The Late 1940s and 1950s


			A New Surge of Interest


			A resurgence of interest in countertransference occurred in Great Britain and the United States in the late 1940s and early 1950s, in two parallel but quite different psychoanalytic movements. In postwar Britain, psychoanalysis was in a state of heated conflict between the followers of Anna Freud (Freud’s daughter and herself a psychoanalyst) and those of Melanie Klein (an analyst who preceded Anna Freud in emigrating to England). Both women were deeply concerned with child analysis. Anna Freud took a more educational approach while Klein believed that even very small children could be analyzed. “Kleinians” were also more preoccupied with very early emotional neglect and damage, so they tended to see more severely disturbed and regressive patients. As a result, these analysts were confronted with much more intense countertransference reactions. Their need to understand and make use of these strong feelings toward their patients eventually resulted in the newer intersubjective conceptualization of countertransference as the sum of the helper’s responses toward the helpee.

			Klein herself had little or no interest in countertransference since her own focus was almost entirely on the inner fantasy life of her patients. But Klein introduced a concept that soon became central to the evolution of the concept of countertransference by mid-century—the concept of “projective identification.” Two terms become important here. “Projection” itself is the unconscious process whereby one individual “projects” or throws an image (usually of a person or experience from early in life) onto another, as a movie is projected onto a screen. Individuals then see the other person through this projected image, which may bear much or only a little resemblance to the real other person behind the screen. They may then find themselves reacting in irrational or disproportionate ways toward the person on the receiving end of the projection, based on what they perceive in the projection on the “screen,” not what the other is actually thinking, feeling, or doing.

			Projective identification takes this projective process one step further. In projective identification, it is as if the projected image is no longer simply projected onto the other individual, as if onto a screen, but into the other, where it is actually taken inside and identified with, by both the patient and the helper. When on the receiving end of such an intense projective process, we then may actually begin to feel and to behave in the ways the patient expected, because we have unconsciously identified with the projection and begun to act it out.

			In reflecting upon this situation, the receiving individuals often can recognize that it feels as if something alien had been put into them, and that they are behaving in ways that are atypical for them. Some forms of projective identification will, however, be more congenial or “ego syntonic” to the recipient than others. The greatest danger lies in a projective identification that is so closely linked to the receiving individual’s own unprocessed inner tendencies that it goes unrecognized as stimulation from the other. The resulting interlocking pattern of unconscious actions and reactions can create an intense and mutually destructive pattern of habitual relating between the two individuals involved.

			This is why self-reflection is so important whenever a particularly intense feeling arises in relationship to another person. Such introspection is not innate but is learned, probably most effectively through personal psychotherapy. It can also be learned or reinforced in personal situations by getting “reality checks” from friends and family, or in professional situations, from professional training, individual or group supervision (as in CPE), and/or confidential consultation from colleagues. It is in fact incumbent upon helping professionals to engage in this type of introspection. But such reflection is almost always helpful in all intimate relationships and can be especially valuable in couples’ work.

			In Klein’s usage, the term “projective identification” was reserved for the effect of split-off archaic inner objects on the analyst in the patient’s fantasy only. However, Klein’s followers soon appropriated this concept in connection with the intense real-life effect patients have on their analysts.
