You know, all the usual stuff, lipstick stains, a receipt from the jewelry store for something I’ve certainly never received from him. And what’s really killing me is that I think the kids are starting to suspect, too. In fact, knowing Emily and Peter, they probably knew before I did.” She smiled ruefully. “It’s all so trite, I hate myself for even telling you any of this. It’s like right out of a soap opera!”

			“But it hurts, Elaine, doesn’t it?” Terence murmured. She nodded, and new tears sprang to her eyes. Terence got up from his chair and paced the room once, then came back and sat next to Elaine on the couch. “The question is,” he said, looking deeply into her eyes, “what do you want your life to be about? Is this the way you want to live? Is this the way God wants you to live?”

			She shook her head. “I knew you would know what to say,” she said, returning his gaze. “You always know what to say.”

			“God wants you to have the love you deserve,” Terence said, more forcefully. He took a tissue and dabbed at Elaine’s eyes. “Remember, Jesus said, ‘I came that they might have life and have it abundantly.’”

			“I wish I could really believe that, Terry. But you know, I don’t think Jim has, you know, really wanted me for a long time, since my lupus got worse and I started to limp. I mean, who could blame him?” Elaine felt her face flush.

			“You’re a beautiful woman, Elaine. Don’t you know that?” Elaine teared up again. Terence turned toward Elaine and leaned in a bit closer. “You can believe in Jesus’s promise of the abundant life. You just have to claim the promise. So, what are you going to do about this?” He looked again into her eyes and thought how soft they looked, not steely and sharp the way Jane’s always seemed of late.

			“Well,” Elaine said slowly, “I keep telling myself I have to let Jim know that I’m not fooled anymore by his lies and his empty words about how he still loves me ‘more than ever.’ I need to stand up to him and tell him that either he stops this or I’m going to see a lawyer. I’m going to get a divorce, Terry. I know it will be hell on the kids and me, but it’s the right thing to do.”

			“You have to trust that inner voice,” Terence replied, and he drew Elaine into a sensuous, enveloping hug.

			Elaine pulled back, suddenly startled by the feelings she was having toward Terence. “We shouldn’t be doing this, Terence,” she said in a shaky voice.

			“Doing what, Elaine?” Terence pulled back slightly, looking hurt. “Can’t a pastor give a parishioner a healthy hug?”

			Elaine looked down at the shredded tissue in her hand. “Of course,” she said. “How stupid of me. Now I’m embarrassed all over again. Maybe I’m imagining all kinds of things—with Jim, too. Maybe it’s me who’s really just not in love with Jim anymore.”

			“Don’t be embarrassed,” Terence said soothingly. “Your emotions are all churned up right now, that’s all. I think it would be a good idea if we schedule a weekly appointment to do some counseling around this. My sense is that there are early-childhood issues at work in you. Doesn’t it feel like some deep things are getting tapped in you?”

			Elaine couldn’t disagree with that. She nodded.

			“Good! That’s an important step. Now be a good girl and go home and think about what we’ve been talking about. You can set up a weekly time in my calendar with Betty on your way out. And while you’re at it, why don’t you ask Betty for a copy of that sermon? She always keeps a stack since I get so many requests.”





Entering Further into the Case Study


			It might be easy to dislike Terence based on this version of the case vignette. Terence has made an egregious mistake and is sliding fast down the “slippery slope” of sexual boundary violation.31 While Terence himself considers that he has done nothing wrong,32 he has already sexualized the pastoral relationship through inappropriate touch and physical closeness. Out of the confusion and pain Elaine is feeling about her marriage, she has idealized Terence and even perhaps signaled sexual attraction to him. In contrast to the emotional distance she feels from her husband, Terence’s seemingly gentle attention feels like a balm to her wounds. This is not at all to say that Elaine is therefore to blame for the crossing of sexual boundaries between them. The all-too-common stereotype of the divorced (or divorcing) female seductress and the myth of the pastor as a “sitting duck” for women’s sexual wiles33 are both forms of blaming the victim that ignore the professional ethics of care.34 It is Terence’s responsibility as the professional caregiver, not Elaine’s, to monitor the boundary and to maintain a safe, facilitative environment for Elaine to explore her pastoral needs.35

			Elaine’s idealization, which is a normal transference reaction during a time of physical, sexual, and emotional duress, actually signals her need for healthy, safe pastoral support to help her gain a sense of direction and purpose in her marital relationship. It likely further signals her distress about her lupus diagnosis, her growing disability, and her fear of the future progression of the disease. Terence mistakes this transference communication for an invitation to enact a sexual encounter. He fails to attend to the deeper meaning suggested by her communications. He then takes advantage of the authority inherent in the clergy role and the power of her idealization to enact a sexual fantasy rather than explore the actual needs present. He rationalizes his behavior both to Elaine and to himself under the guise of “giving a parishioner a healthy hug.” This masking of the inappropriate enactment—an example of “gaslighting”36—further confuses Elaine and increases Terence’s power and control in the situation. But in this version of the vignette, Terence has been attempting to meet his own needs, not Elaine’s. Terence has already abused Elaine, “grooming” her for further possible sexual exploitation.





Lack of Self-Care and Risk Factors


			It is clear, perhaps, from the opening paragraphs of Terence’s vignette in the introduction that Terence is already at risk for some form of professional misconduct. Terence is himself experiencing stress and conflict in his marriage. He appears to be dealing with this by “stonewalling,”37 literally shutting out the sound of his wife’s voice and externalizing the blame for their difficulties entirely onto her, rather than considering what his own shared responsibility might be. At the same time, he is indulging in fantasies about the parish treasurer, Susan. He disavows any direct wrongdoing with Susan, while secretly enjoying the feelings her pleasant manner arouses. Terence has unconsciously engaged in a form of splitting, in which his wife represents the bad woman in his life, Susan represents the good, and he experiences himself as an innocent victim of pressure, external demands, and conflict. This unconscious splitting impedes intentional, thoughtful self-care by displacing healthy needs for self-centering awareness onto others from whom he compulsively seeks gratification.

			Terence’s ambition also suggests a narcissistic vulnerability. Being on the short list for bishop is both stimulating and anxiety-provoking for Terence because he not only feels it is a challenging and exciting call to ministry, but at a deeper level, feels tantalized by the promise of affirmation and admiration he inwardly craves. He has already depended on external symbols of wealth and power, such as his luxury car, and has sought a “high steeple” church position that would reinforce his self-image as powerful and successful. Terence’s narcissistic vulnerability is a significant risk factor for boundary violations, following the profile of many clergy offenders.38

			From the very outset of this case study, then, several significant risk factors are present. Risk factors, however, in and of themselves do not always result in inappropriate behavior. The single most important predictor for any form of abuse is past behavior. Once a boundary has been crossed, rehabilitation is much more difficult, and prognosis is almost always poor.39 Reinstatement in ministry or other helping professions at this point is a high-risk proposition. The fact of having crossed the line from fantasy into actual behavior demonstrates a level of impulsivity that signals a deeper entrenchment of narcissistic character pathology.

			Terence’s behavior with Elaine in the vignette has crossed this line in several ways, some quite subtle. From the very first moment in which Terence takes Elaine’s cane, Terence conveys paternalistic power over Elaine in the guise of kindness and pastoral concern. He does a number of things Elaine could be expected to do for herself, including positioning her cane, reaching for the tissue box even though it is closer to her, and telling her to ask for a copy of his sermon. His language, especially calling her a “good girl,” is patronizing and disempowering.

			These pseudo-solicitous acts of power are combined with an increasing physical proximity, beginning with rolling his chair close to the couch, presumably leaning across her to reach the tissues, then sitting next to her on the couch, and finally drawing her into an “enveloping hug.” Only when Elaine pulls away does Terence quickly disavow his exploitative behaviors, reframing them as appropriate pastoral care and comfort.

			Terence has behaved exploitatively toward Elaine, although his actions are ambiguous enough that Elaine would have difficulty proving misconduct if she ever attempted to bring ecclesiastical charges or legal action. Terence would deny any impropriety, likely claiming that Elaine had misconstrued his pastoral warmth as something more. His narcissistic vulnerability would cause him to believe his own protestations, needing as he does to be in the superior position, and would make him a believable witness in his own defense. Elaine would be blamed or even pathologized for bringing the complaint. Why is Terence able to believe he is innocent or even, if threatened with the truth of his actions, believe he is the one who was victimized by Elaine?

			Terence failed to engage in any sort of self-reflection during Elaine’s office visit and gave in to his own powerful, only partially conscious, needs and feelings. In the office with Elaine, Terence chose enactment over verbal understanding, because in some sense it was easier than facing his own painful inner reality. He used words skillfully, but his use of empathy was directed toward a careful step-by-step seduction of Elaine toward meeting his own sexual and emotional needs.40 This was the way the ethical violation unfolded. But recognizing the ethical failure as such, and the exploitation of the power dynamics inherent in the helper-helpee relationship, does not explain why Terence failed, nor why, if confronted later, he would be vehement in defending himself with a clear conscience. To understand the why, it is necessary to understand Terence’s underlying psychological makeup. The emotional exploitation of others described in the vignette is the hallmark of narcissistic pathology.

			Narcissism is not simply conceit or self-absorption, as it is understood in the popular sense. The symptomatic behaviors of narcissism involve an inflated self-regard, ambition exceeding one’s abilities (although many narcissistically wounded individuals are high achievers because of their inner drive for affirmation), and a strong sense of entitlement. Narcissistically wounded persons believe at the preconscious level that worldly benefits should accrue, and rules should not apply, because they are exceptional and deserving of special consideration and rewards. Conversations, agendas, plans, and behaviors generally revolve around getting other people to praise and affirm the narcissist. There is a heavy wall of denial and resistance to seeing beneath this preconscious self-regard because the acknowledgment of narcissistic desires is almost always laden with shame.41 However, carefully guarded beneath this preconscious self-absorption, there generally exists an unconscious psychological void. In the right kind of empathic psychodynamic psychotherapy, narcissistically wounded individuals may gradually peel off the mask of grandiosity and uncover deep depressive feelings of emptiness, worthlessness, and despair. This, in turn, requires further therapy to address these painful emotions.

			There are various psychological theories on the origins of such narcissistic wounding.42 Simply put, most theories agree that the narcissistic individual did not receive the admiration and unconditional loving praise that was needed very early in life. Around the first birthday, a child begins to walk and talk and to enter into human community in new and powerful ways. When this is appropriately mirrored,43 the child begins to build within themself a well of self-confidence and a healthy pride in accomplishment. This begins even in infancy, as infants internalize their capacity to make an impact on the big people in their environment. But when such basic needs are not met, sometimes even at a very subtle level, the well remains empty. The infant’s actions are not experienced as having impact. This can happen in a number of ways. Most often, it occurs because primary caretakers are too preoccupied by their own stresses and external pressures to adequately affirm the child’s developmental achievements. More pathologically, they may unconsciously appropriate the child’s successes to prop up their own fragile ego needs, thus treating the child as a narcissistic extension of themselves.44

			Eventually, the empty well of affirmation comes to be sealed over in the unconscious by a heavy veneer of performance-oriented behavior. But because the original parental situation did not fill up the child’s internal place of self-confidence, the thirst for praise and attention begins to function something like an addiction. Applause and promotion become primary motivations for one’s life choices and activities. Such children grow up learning how to read others acutely, in order to recruit praise and avoid criticism. Criticism is dreaded, because it threatens to reveal the emptiness felt within, the unconscious but ever-present sense of oneself as an imposter, and is thus laden with the potential for arousing primal feelings of shame. Empathy is thus skewed toward a form of self-­preservation that uses others’ feelings as a barometer for one’s own self-advancement.

			At the heart of the problem of narcissism, then, is not the lack of capacity for empathy per se. Many pathologically serial offenders manipulate others sexually, financially, or in other ways precisely by their finely tuned ability to read the needs and feelings of others. There is, however, in such entrenched cases of narcissism, a serious lack of empathy for one’s impact on others and an underlying arrest in moral development to the stage where “what’s right is what I want.”

			Not all narcissistically wounded individuals behave that exploitatively. Some theorists now suggest that just as neurosis was considered universal in the earlier part of the twentieth century, narcissistic character pathology may be equally ubiquitous in late-twentieth-to early-twenty-first-century Western civilization.45 Once an actual boundary violation has occurred, however, and especially in the case of repeated violations, the level of narcissistic pathology must be viewed as more deeply entrenched and problematic. This degree of narcissism is on the extreme end of the spectrum toward sociopathy. Remorse becomes impossible, because one’s own needs are so overwhelming that others’ needs or the impact of one’s own actions cannot even be seen, except in a view distorted by self-interest.

			Terence, like Gary, has been seduced by the self-image of “special carer” and hero to his “flock.” Terence’s internal needs are quite different, however, and more active disavowal is at work. Terence can best be described in terms of what is known in self psychology as a “vertical split.” The concept of the vertical split was first introduced into self psychology by Heinz Kohut in his paper “Two Analyses of Mr. Z.”46 The vertical split is conceived as a psychological mechanism of “disavowal” (rather than “repression,” as in either Freud’s topographical model or later structural model).47 Unlike the process of repression, in which intolerable mental contents are understood to be pushed downward out of consciousness into a deeper realm of the unconscious where they are virtually inaccessible to consciousness, in the process of disavowal, certain aspects of oneself are accessible to consciousness but are so uncomfortable to one’s own sense of self that they are normally kept out of awareness.

			The difference between these two forms of splitting—repression and disavowal—is illustrated in Kohut’s paper (see Figure 4.2).48 The “vertical split” is so named because it divides consciousness into two areas, pictured on the same horizontal plane, which are not entirely inaccessible to each other. In contrast, in the horizontal layers of the topographical model, the deepest layer, the unconscious, is separated by a repression barrier that is impermeable (without help).



					Figure 4.2 The Vertical Split.



			The vertical split is usually identified with narcissistic pathology and is mainly manifested in behaviors, often compulsive acts that the person would ordinarily find completely alien. Thus, rather than splitting the personality into conscious and unconscious domains (as in classically defined neuroses), the vertical split is conceived to explain patterns of inconsistent behavior, in which different behavior patterns are governed by the two separate, different arenas of consciousness, and by corresponding inhibitions or disinhibitions. Individuals whose subjective experience resonates closely with this concept of the vertical split often describe this much in the same terms as the apostle Paul’s self-­description in Romans 7: “I do not understand my own actions . . . For I do not do the good I want, but the evil I do not want is what I do” (Rom 7:15, 19).

			The vertical split has its origins, according to self psychological theory, in the lack of parental provisions as “selfobjects,” reliable (or unreliable) and internalizable figures upon whom the individual depended in childhood. Inadequate or inconsistent parenting over a period of crucial early years results in weak or lacking internal psychological structure, particularly on two poles:49 On the “mirroring” pole, the child should receive recognition, warmth, unconditional affirmation, “the gleam in the parent’s eye.” On the “idealizing” pole, the child experiences the parental selfobjects as reliable, admirable, and idealizable—the core of values around which adult aspirations and ideals are formed. When all goes well, mirroring allows infantile grandiosity to be fed and gradually modified into a mature sense of affirmed identity, self-confidence, and appropriate pride in one’s realistic capacities and achievements.

			Likewise, idealizing allows infantile dependence to evolve into mature interdependence in relationships, and pursuit of goals founded in a secure sense of ideals, values, and purpose. When all does not go well, however, the result is an inner experience of fragmentation, lack, and depressive emptiness, a sense that inside oneself “there is no there there.” This deep insecurity is disguised by an external, or social presentation of inflated self-importance, sensitivity to criticism, and narcissistic grandiosity and entitlement.50

			With the vertical split phenomenon, the external presentation is the “me” that narcissistically wounded individuals identify with, often quite successful in some arenas, but also frequently unable to form genuine intimate bonds in relationships and baffled by the reluctance of others in one’s world to recognize their superiority. On this side of the split, they find themselves attempting to ward off depression and at times flying into inexplicable rages that are out of proportion to a perceived threat or criticism, while unable to soothe themselves or restore a mature sense of perspective.

			To paraphrase the fourth century African theologian Augustine, narcissistically wounded persons become “problems to themselves.”51 As Augustine wrote in his Confessions, “I find my own self hard to grasp. I have become for myself a soil which is the cause of much difficulty and sweat.”52 When productive activity and the release of tension through rages fail to soothe, then narcissistically wounded persons slip over onto the other side of the split and engage in compulsive behaviors in a frantic, joyless attempt at filling the inner sense of emptiness, or in its most sociopathic form, “the world owes me” (what Winnicott termed “the antisocial tendency”).53 Obsessive, self-centered sexual behaviors, alcohol and drug abuse, gambling addiction, physical violence, and other behaviors destructive of self, others, or both can often be understood dynamically as behavioral evidence of this vertical split born of early narcissistic deficit or injury. The predominant affect associated with those moments in which the individual is aware of this “other side” of the vertical split is not guilt, as with neurotic behaviors, but profound shame: not “I did something bad,” but “I am bad.”





Classical Countertransference


			How might an awareness of Terence’s countertransference feelings have prevented the unethical behavior described in the vignette and led to a more constructive pastoral encounter with Elaine? Let us rewind the scenario to the point where Terence is leaving the house for the day and let us further assume that Terence has not yet actually violated any boundaries.

			It is important to note that the presence of sexual feelings does not automatically define a pastor as a sexual predator! A wide range of feelings is normal when working intensively with people around emotional and spiritual issues. However, acting on all feelings is not normal or appropriate. The capacity to become aware of one’s own feelings, and then to consider in a reflective vein what such feelings might be communicating—about both one’s own needs for healing and the needs of the helpee—are the essential skills for maintaining ethical conduct for caring genuinely and empathically for another.

			In Terence’s case, before he would be in a position to safely help Elaine, a number of things would already need to be in place for him, including individual psychotherapy, spiritual direction, marriage counseling, and professional consultation. In the ideal, these would not all be simultaneous, but sequential, and should have begun years before the present situation.

			The first and most urgent recommendation for Terence is individual psychotherapy with a therapist experienced in treating narcissistic pathology and ideally familiar with clergy boundary issues. The level of narcissistic wounding evident in the vignette would not be a recent development in Terence’s life. Failures and losses in adult life, even severe blows to one’s self-confidence, do not result in the type of disavowal and exploitative behaviors described in this story. Such a pattern of entitlement, unconscious need for acclaim, and exploitation of others must have originated in early childhood, as described in the previous section. For this reason, the pattern should have been evident, at least in some form, before Terence was ever ordained to the ministry. In the ideal, concerns about Terence’s need to see himself as super-successful, and possible clues to issues with entitlement or lack of appropriate boundaries should have become evident to someone who had authority in Terence’s ordination process: a perceptive ordination committee, psychological evaluator, CPE or field education supervisor, or seminary faculty. It would be important for these concerns to be surfaced early, before Terence is ordained, and while others still have a significant amount of authority to guide his learning and supervise his behavior.54

			Red flags that can help with recognizing and addressing narcissistic vulnerability can include an attraction by the candidate to assignments perceived as “glamorous, dramatic, or dangerous.”55 Such candidates do not like jobs they perceive as beneath their talents, preferring settings where they will be perceived as powerful and in the limelight. There is a strong tendency toward autonomous action and resistance to true accountability. When pressed on issues of accountability by a church official or other ecclesiastical authority, usually the candidate (while perhaps feigning taking responsibility) will show resistance to self-examination and may have difficulty taking responsibility for past questionable behaviors without excuse or qualification.56 There is frequently a tendency to frame wants as needs, to frame conflictual situations in terms of being victimized, and to deny having caused others any harm. Because manipulation and the projection of a star image are common in narcissistically wounded individuals, empathy and conscience are often convincingly feigned. But at the core of the person’s self-experience, there is often overwhelming despair, emptiness, fear, and easily triggered rage. For this reason, there is often difficulty establishing appropriate intimate relationships and friendships with peers, leading to a “lone ranger” style of ministry.57 Other people are used compulsively and heedlessly in a desperate attempt to keep the demons of worthlessness at bay.

			If this vulnerability had been recognized early enough, Terence’s ordination could have been put on hold, and an appropriate course of psychotherapy recommended. Even long-term psychotherapy cannot guarantee that an individual with a severe level of narcissistic wounding will ever be a safe candidate for ministry. But in many cases, sufficiently deep and intensive therapy can uncover and begin to heal the pathology, with one of two results: Such individuals may be able to recognize their own vulnerabilities and choose a different profession in which their needs can be met in a healthy way without putting others at risk for exploitation. Or their grandiose needs can be modified and met sufficiently that they are no longer likely to seek inappropriate support and affirmation from those in their care.

			Without such intervention, there is, sadly, little hope that an individual can break out of this entrenched pattern of need and use of others. The antisocial tendency compulsively drives the individual toward committing acts aimed at filling the internal void. This is futile, however. We cannot fill ourselves up. The most effective remedy is probably a process Kohut called “transmuting internalization,”58 whereby relationships with new selfobjects facilitate the process of healthy structuralization. Empathic ruptures in the therapeutic relationship are inevitable. But when followed by the therapist’s responsiveness—very different from one’s early childhood experiences of non-responsiveness—growth or repair of one’s inner self-structure can gradually take place. Through this process, given enough time, the vertical split with its accompanying unconscious habit of disavowal may gradually be recognized consciously and healed. In this way, one’s behavior can gradually change from shame-laden, compulsive, hidden activity driven by a need for grandiose self-inflation and self-soothing, to behavior that is more integrated and genuinely relational.

			The original damage is done. What was lacking in earliest childhood at the appropriate time cannot simply be filled in later in life once the early developmental moment has passed—what some therapists refer to as “re-parenting” or a “corrective emotional experience.” The original damage cannot be undone—it must be recognized and mourned. Transmuting internalization, however, functions in a way like grace: it does not replace the original goodness that was intended and then lost in creation, but it does mediate a restoration of the capacity, however imperfect, to desire the good once again. While this process of transmuting internalization may not in every case require the services of an analyst, the aim of any psychodynamic psychotherapy, as well as the potentially curative function of all loving relationships, is in the healing of the vertical split and the restoration of wholeness. This corresponds to the theological notion of the healing power of grace.

			To the extent that self psychologists increasingly view this conception of psychopathology as narcissistic deficit to be more or less true for everyone, this model might even correspond with the Christian theological construct of original sin. No one gets out of childhood with a perfectly shored up self-structure, so no one can escape the propensity toward self-inflation and narcissistic vulnerability in one form or another (or in theological terms, we are all heirs to the brokenness of the human condition).

			Does this mean that every candidate for ministry should engage in some course of individual psychotherapy to address narcissistic vulnerability? It couldn’t hurt! But certainly, this case study emphasizes the importance of careful psychological screening of candidates, erring on the side of urging all candidates to engage in a compassionately supervised process of guided introspection and self-care. As I have argued elsewhere, the profession of ministry inherently attracts individuals who likely hold a self-image as rescuer, protector, or caretaker. In Terence’s story, Elaine’s vulnerability is a significant element in Terence’s attraction to her, not despite, but because of, her tears and physical disability.59 Again, larger issues of social and political context are relevant in this dynamic. Female gender, race, physical ability, and other demographic characteristics that are vulnerable to oppression may constitute elements that stir the narcissistic wounded healer’s compulsion to rescue—and simultaneously to exploit.

			This is where committees screening candidates for ministry can be honestly confused by narcissistically wounded candidates for ministry. A candidate’s tendency—or compulsion—to care for others may result in laudable altruistic activities, even a gender-socialized appearance of heroism and self-sacrifice, especially in men, or a high (even excessive) level of nurturing and compassion in women. But, as has been discussed at length in this chapter, the outward appearance of heroism, self-sacrifice, or compulsive caretaking is, in fact, a red flag for narcissistic vulnerability. While classical psychoanalysts might dismiss any notion of genuine heroism,60 many religious traditions lift up spiritual heroes, forebears, or “saints.” An important distinguishing mark of genuine saintliness, however, like true heroism, is not eagerness but “reluctance.” A genuine vocation is often marked by authentic protest of one’s incapacity, and genuine courage is marked by the presence, not absence, of fear—and most individuals later identified as “saints” are rarely perfect people. One does what one is called to do, despite feeling unworthy, inadequate, and afraid. This is the opposite of narcissistic striving, which is marked by bravado, a surface self-confidence, and sense of entitlement to an exalted status.

			This leads to a second recommendation, that of spiritual direction. “Discernment of spirits” is necessary, both by candidacy committees and by the candidate themself. Spiritual direction cannot fruitfully be engaged in before serious psychological wounding has been addressed and the individual is well on the path to recovery. However, at a certain point well along in the healing process, the issue of spiritual and vocational discernment should be incorporated into one’s personal work, either through psychodynamic pastoral counseling in which the spiritual issues are integrated or through a separate course of spiritual direction with a psychologically well-trained, and (ideally) well-supervised director.61

			Spiritual direction can help address issues of vocational discernment and a “testing of spirits.” A genuine calling may, in fact, include mixed motives, both worldly and “not of this world.” By building a consistent discipline of listening prayer, one’s sense of ministry should gradually become more genuinely other-­centered, and less “me”-centered. Such contemplative practices of centering prayer and meditation allow one to open one’s mind to the grace that is already given, creating space for Spirit to enter into one’s heart with healing and transforming power.

			A regular practice of prayer or meditation can also have the effect of slowing one down and creating the introspection and calm necessary to interrupt compulsive behaviors and develop a greater capacity for self-observation without the kind of neurotic guilt or shame that drives self-knowledge back into the regions of the unconscious. A genuine building of connectedness with God or the Holy in prayer or meditation can begin to fill the depressive, shame-laden void of narcissism in a way that no other relationship can do, because contemplative practices put one in contact with the compassionate love and acceptance of the divine. C. S. Lewis, in The Problem of Pain, named compulsive, addictive behaviors as human beings’ misguided efforts to fill the “hole that only God can fill.”62

			Perhaps God’s mercy and judgment are not opposites, as is commonly thought. Psychoanalyst and spiritual director Phillip Bennett has written:

			Given the destructive nature of our self-judgment, what are we to make of all the images of God’s judgment in our religious scriptures and traditions? How can we reconcile God’s love with God’s judgment? The most satisfying answer I have found is that God’s judgment is God’s love, in its penetrating, unremitting power. God’s judgment is never divorced from God’s love; it is not some angry part of God, which is split off from God’s mercy and gentleness. Instead, God’s judgment is the way we experience pure and constant love that sees and knows us to our core. Being known so deeply is like [a] golf ball: our layers of self-deception and avoidance of intimacy must be unwound until love can touch us to our core.63

			The third recommendation for Terence is marriage counseling. Assuming that Terence has done the work of psychotherapy and spiritual discernment during his ordination process and is no longer at risk for severe narcissistically exploitative behavior, conflict or stress in one’s own marriage is still a risk factor for failing to provide appropriate pastoral care, especially to a parishioner experiencing marital difficulties. Terence’s stonewalling with his wife Jane is likely part of a larger pattern of difficulties in communication that have been building for some time. If, in spite of working through personal issues in his own therapy, Terence nevertheless finds himself indulging in fantasies about the parish treasurer, Susan, this is likely a sign that something is being unaddressed in his own marriage. Family therapists are in general agreement that having an affair, or fantasizing about having one, is not the primary cause of damage in a marriage, but rather a symptom.

			The form of psychological splitting in which Terence is unconsciously engaging, in which his wife is all bad and Susan is all good, suggests a dynamic in which Terence is waging some form of internal battle. Often, the most negative trait one identifies in one’s partner is, in fact, a negative trait one wishes to disavow in oneself. By externalizing certain negatives onto Jane, such as considering her to be controlling or demanding, Terence has managed to blame Jane rather than owning the controlling and demanding inner voices that plague him from within. Often, these inner voices are, again, voices from early childhood, internalized as a part of oneself but largely operating at the unconscious level. Couples therapy, particularly with a psychodynamic focus,64 should help Terence and Jane not only to improve their communication style but also to withdraw the (likely mutual) projections that are impeding their capacity for a conscious, loving relationship. Terence can withdraw the projection of a critical, demanding controller from Jane and own it as part of himself. At the same time, he can withdraw the projection of the fantasied ideal woman from Susan and recognize those positive aspects in Jane that caused him to love her in the first place. Especially if Jane can meet him halfway in this project, they can open their communication to share their vulnerabilities with each other. This should help them to reconnect in a supportive way, with each partner more able to hold good and bad together in a more realistic perception of the other.

			This, in turn, will greatly help Terence’s capacity to listen helpfully to Elaine. Rather than jumping to take Elaine’s side in a split against her husband, Terence may be able to offer a more thoughtful approach that respects both partners. Because he no longer needs to side with Elaine as a tactic to rescue or seduce her and is also no longer projecting his own marital splits into the marriage of Elaine and Jim, he can participate in an appropriate brief, cognitive-style care-and-counseling model aimed primarily at assessment and referral. This can help Elaine explore the possibility of marital counseling with her husband, or failing that, individual therapy for herself.

			Finally, as in the case of Gary, Terence would do well to seek professional consultation and/or peer supervision. Supervision can help Terence see dimensions of the transference-countertransference dynamic he is missing, and also steer him back to practices of self-care and honest self-examination. Of course, supervision undertaken with a genuine desire for honest feedback is the first line of defense against unconscious impulses toward grandiose behavior, in the form either of heroism or of getting one’s personal needs met through a parishioner. Supervision can catch tendencies to project an attitude of “I can do anything; rules don’t apply.”

			Nor are sexual feelings in and of themselves signals of narcissistic pathology. Healthy clergypersons also on occasion experience sexual feelings toward congregation members. In a healthy or “good-enough” situation,65 these feelings can be acknowledged rather than disavowed and dealt with as useful information about the intensity of the pastoral relationship, rather than as an invitation to become sexually or romantically involved. Sexual feelings should automatically signal a need for consultation—not because they are bad or a sign of one’s being a bad religious leader. On the contrary, they signal a need for supervision because they herald significant developments in the unconscious relationship between the clergyperson and congregation member that require a third professional perspective. This third perspective is not only for the purpose of avoiding “the slippery slope” of inappropriate enactments, but also to help the pastor use these feelings to choose appropriate interventions66 and imagine empathically what these feelings might mean about the legitimate needs and possible suffering of the parishioner.





Preliminary Pastoral Assessment


			Let us assume that Terence has fully engaged in forms of counseling, spiritual care, and supervision as recommended in the previous section, both in the past and in the present. All this should help him recognize elements in Elaine’s presentation that would enable him to make a caring, appropriate response. Having come through his own marital therapy to understand the power of mutual projection and splitting, Terence may consider that some of this same common process may be occurring with Elaine and Jim. He should notice Elaine’s idealization of him as part of her transference to him as helper and, further, be alert to the likelihood of unconscious splitting. In the absence of any evidence that Jim is abusive, in which case he would need to attend Elaine’s safety and refer her confidentially to a battered women’s organization for specialized support and advocacy,67 Terence should be alert to the possibility that he is standing in for the “good husband” and her husband Jim is being seen as all “bad.” Jim may be projecting some of his own internal issues onto Elaine as well, and perhaps as Elaine’s illness has progressed, tension caused by this has exacerbated longer-­standing issues in their relationship. Jim has been unable to support Elaine fully, and for whatever reason, the couple has not been able to work through this with effective emotional communication.

			Terence’s goal in this situation, then, is not to try to “fix” Elaine’s problem or even to alleviate her personal suffering directly. Rather, his goal is to listen to Elaine empathically, validate her feelings, help her identify what healthy changes might be possible, and refer her to the right kind of professional support to facilitate those changes. Such an approach also, by putting the power for decision-­making back in Elaine’s own hands, empowers Elaine instead of patronizing or pathologizing her.





Intersubjective Countertransference and Further Pastoral Assessment


			An examination of Terence’s countertransference responses, as in the cases of Linda and Gary, can also do more than simply prevent inappropriate pastoral intrusions. If Terence can recognize his sexual feelings for Elaine consciously (so that they are not acted upon), but also without shame or self-indictment, he may be able to gain a deeper insight into Elaine’s own suffering. Erotic elements in the transference-countertransference dynamic often signal more than sexual desire, frustration, or loneliness. Feminist theologians have convincingly noted and critiqued a pervasive denigration of sexuality and the body in classical theology, inherited from Greek mind-body dualism. Eros has been reclaimed in the affirmative sense as the life force, even the creative energy of God’s own self.68 The presence of eros in the intersubjective space between helper and helpee may signal not only sexual desire per se, but a deeper desire for life itself in all its possible excitement and fullness.

			What might this mean specifically in Elaine’s situation? Elaine is struggling not only with unhappiness in her marriage, but also with the reality of having lupus, a progressive disease. While there are many things Elaine can do to manage her illness and enjoy a good quality of life, she is no longer able to take her health for granted, as perhaps she once did before her diagnosis. Living with the reality of a progressive disability, Elaine must contend with her body, including issues of sexual desire and desirability, procreation, and mortality, in ways that many non-disabled individuals typically deny. She also may represent to other (temporarily) able-bodied persons the vulnerability of the body and the fragility of life.69 For this reason, she may encounter unconscious hostility and projection of others’ fears of illness and mortality onto her. This may, in fact, be a dynamic at work in the problems of her marriage.

			Terence’s sexual feelings toward Elaine may also reflect Elaine’s unconscious desires as an embodied expression of the intersubjective countertransference, in addition to quasi-fulfilling his own embodied longing for more excitement, more real intimacy in his own life. If Terence can recognize and utilize this possible dimension of his countertransference, he may be better able to help Elaine recognize and even celebrate the embodied life she is living, disability and all. By refusing either to deny, to sexualize, or to project his own fears onto Elaine’s illness, Terence can stand ready to hear some of the underlying existential fears that Elaine is bringing into this pastoral relationship. By refusing to participate actively in a dynamic of splitting, at least to the extent that he can be aware of it, Terence can use his responses to hold up both positive and negative dimensions of Elaine’s experience, of the marital dynamic, and of her struggles with her disability.





Theological/Spiritual Reflection


			The biblical image of the “abundant life,” which in the original vignette Terence used to join with one side of the Elaine-Jim split, exploit her sense of vulnerability with her disability, and seduce her, can now be understood as representing the true promise of God to all the parties in this story: Elaine, Jim, their children, and Terence and Jane themselves. The Gospel text from John 10:10, which surfaced in Terence’s consciousness in connection with Elaine’s suffering, applies to all of them equally: “I came that they might have life, and have it abundantly.” The sacred promise of life—embodied, relational, and real, can prevent Terence both from splitting off his desires and from acting out compulsively to fill his own needs at the expense of his parishioners. This grace, freely poured out for the sake of the whole world, can then be made available freely to Elaine, not as a tantalizing fantasy of escape, but as an affirmation of her life as it is, both wonderful and terrible, with all its true potential for personal healing and for the healing of her marriage and family relationships.

			What if all these insights had been available to Terence? In the following reworking of the vignette, notice, as with Linda, that Terence is not now the “perfect pastor.” He experiences discomfort and temptation. The key differences have to do with Terence’s being able to become aware of his feelings in the moment, to recognize them for what they are likely signaling him about Elaine’s needs, rather than joining with Elaine’s idealization of him and forgetting to address the wider needs of Elaine’s whole family. He is also more able to be receptive to the possibility that Elaine’s disability is at the heart of at least some of her present suffering. His awareness of this enables him to treat her as a whole person, respectfully, avoiding being paternalistic or over-solicitous, while at the same time being alert to themes of existential vulnerability and mortality.





Terence, Redux


			Terence takes a deep breath and centers himself for a moment, then ushers Elaine to a seat on the couch in his office. He waits respectfully while Elaine stows her cane under the sofa. Then, drawing his rolling executive chair out from behind his large desk, he sits at a comfortable distance opposite Elaine and indicates the box of tissues on the polished mahogany side table next to her left elbow. “How can I be of help, Elaine?” he asks in a gentle voice. Elaine begins to speak and then bends over and breaks into heavy sobs, her tears falling on her knees. “It’s OK,” Terence says. As Elaine continues to sob, he says, “Elaine, it’s fine to cry. But I’d really like to know what’s bothering you. When you’re ready, can you try to speak? Let’s take a deep breath and count to ten, OK?”

			Elaine nods, takes a shuddering breath, and sits up.

			Terence smiles gently. “OK. Can you tell me what’s happened?”

			Feeling more composed, Elaine begins to speak. “After hearing your sermon Sunday, Terry, I knew I had to come talk with you. Jim still denies that he’s having an affair, but there’s just too much evidence. I . . . I find things. In his car, in the laundry. You know, all the usual stuff, lipstick stains, a receipt from the jewelry store for something I’ve certainly never received from him . . . And what’s really killing me is that I think the kids are starting to suspect, too. In fact, knowing Emily and Peter, they probably knew before I did.” She smiles ruefully. “It’s all so trite, I hate myself for even telling you any of this. It’s like right out of a soap opera!”

			“But it hurts,” Terence said, mirroring the depth of her distress with genuine compassion. She nods, and new tears spring to her eyes. Terence leans back slightly, looking at Elaine directly. “The question is,” he says, conveying respect for her opinion, “what do you want your life to be about? How do you want to live? How do you think God wants you and Jim and the kids to live?”

			She shakes her head. “I knew you would know what to say,” she said, already feeling that things might not be as out of control as they had felt. “You always know what to say.”

			“God wants each of you to have the love you deserve,” Terence says, becoming aware of Elaine’s idealization of him. Trying not to judge himself for the fleeting feeling of enjoyment of Elaine’s admiring gaze, Terence sits up a bit straighter and runs his finger under his clerical collar. “Remember, Elaine, Jesus said, ‘I came that they might have life and have it abundantly.’ And that applies to you.”

			“I wish I could really believe that, Terry. But, you know, I don’t think Jim has, you know, really wanted me for a long time, not since my lupus got worse and I started to limp. I mean, who could blame him?” Elaine felt her face flush.

			Terence feels his own face flush slightly in response and becomes aware of a warm, erotic feeling toward Elaine. “Get a grip, Terry,” he thinks to himself. He conjures Jane’s face in his mind, the faces of his kids, the therapist he consulted for ongoing supervision, even the face of his bishop. Giving in to this feeling would damage all he had been working toward and would also hurt Elaine. “Use this feeling as information,” he thinks to himself. “It’s not about you, so don’t get carried away here! It’s about God, and about life.”

			Taking a deep breath, he sits more squarely in his chair and says matter-of-factly, “You can believe Jesus’s promise, Elaine, for all of you. You just have to claim it. I know you can’t speak for Jim here, or for the kids. You can only really change yourself, as I know you know. But sometimes when one person is determined to make some changes in a really healthy way, the others in the family make important changes, too.”

			Elaine nods. “Yeah, that makes sense. I believe that.”

			“OK, so then the question is, what is a constructive way to make positive change happen? For example, if you woke up tomorrow, and everything was better, what would need to change?”70

			“Well, for one thing, I wouldn’t have lupus!” Elaine says, her face flushing. “But that’s not something I can do much about, other than following doctor’s orders—which I am . . .” Her voice trails off, and her shoulders slump.

			“You seem discouraged.”

			“I am. Sometimes I think I’ve just become my disease. Like there’s nothing else left to me.”

			Terence ponders this. He stops himself from his habitual tendency to want to fix or prematurely reassure his parishioner. “Lupus is a rough thing to deal with,” he acknowledges. They sit in silence for a moment, and one tear slides down Elaine’s cheek.

			Finally, Elaine breaks the silence. “I do wish I could believe in that abundant-­life idea.”

			Terence says, “You know, Elaine, maybe that saying of Jesus can be of some help to you, too, regardless of the lupus. Do you think the abundant life doesn’t apply to you anymore?”

			Elaine takes another deep breath. “I guess I’ve felt pretty abandoned by God. Or punished, even.”

			Terence engages Elaine for a while in conversation about this. Elaine does not exhibit clinical symptoms of depression—change of appetite, loss of sleep, fatigue, lack of enjoyment, disproportionate sadness almost every day, suicidal feelings, or preoccupation with death. Her fear and sadness about her body are objective, related to her diagnosis, and she still has energy to try to address her problems.71 Terence helps Elaine to consider her own beliefs about God, God’s love, and what God wants for her life. Elaine becomes a bit more animated again as she says, “No, I don’t rationally have any reason to believe God is punishing me or that that’s the kind of God I can believe in anyway. I guess it’s possible that God wants me to have an abundant life, even with the lupus. And if I stop trying to pretend, I can make everything go back to the way it was before I got diagnosed, maybe I can find it. Things will be different now, but maybe God can help show it to me.”

			“Yes. I really do believe that, Elaine. Try praying about that, and I will pray for you, too.” Terence feels some of the strain that had been present in the room begin to lift, as Elaine’s shoulders relax and her face brightens ever so slightly.

			“So, then, about Jim . . . ?” she asks.

			“Yes, so, then, what about Jim?” Terence replies.

			“Well, I’ve kept telling myself I have to let Jim know that I’m not fooled anymore by his lies and his empty words about how he still loves me ‘more than ever.’ I need to stand up to him and tell him that either he stops this, or I’m going to see a lawyer. I’m going to get a divorce, Terry. I know it will be hell on the kids and me, but it’s the right thing to do.”

			“Whoa, hold on a minute,” Terence says. “You’ve really said two things. The first is having an honest conversation with Jim. I agree with that, and I think maybe you do need to stand up for yourself, not deny what you feel is true. But it’s quite a leap from there to the divorce lawyer. Is it possible that part of the problem is that you and Jim haven’t been able to have an honest conversation about your relationship? It sounds like that hasn’t been happening in quite a while, at least not from your point of view.”

			Elaine pulls back, suddenly aware of feeling an exciting, wishful desire for Terence. She catches herself in a fleeting fantasy of herself divorcing Jim, Terence divorcing Jane, and the two of them getting together. “No, it hasn’t,” she said, feeling inwardly chastened. “Now I’m embarrassed all over again. Maybe I’m imagining all kinds of things—with Jim, too. Maybe it’s me who’s really just not in love with Jim anymore.”

			“Well, these are some important questions you’re raising here. I don’t think either one of you knows that yet. It sounds like emotions have been running high lately, and the lupus has been a big factor in all of this. The one thing I’m pretty sure I’m hearing is that there hasn’t been enough real communication between you and Jim, maybe for quite a long time. What would you think of the idea of doing some couple’s counseling with him?”

			Elaine agrees, feeling some relief that there might be another solution. But then she hesitated. “I don’t know, though,” she said. “I did suggest counseling once a couple of years ago, and all Jim said was that some radio talk-show guy called counseling the ‘last stop on the road to divorce.’”

			“Well, maybe it’s time to try again. Do you think it would help for the two of you to come in here, and we could talk together about finding the right couples counselor? I know some really good people who have helped other couples’ relationships get a lot stronger.”

			“OK, I’ll give it a try. Would you call Jim and suggest this?”

			Terence ponders Elaine’s request. She looks so vulnerable and lost. He feels his desire to protect and bond with her rising again like a warm tide in his chest. “Red flag,” he thinks, this time smiling slightly at his repeated attempts to outfox himself.

			“I appreciate your trust, Elaine. But I think this is something that will go best if you broach it yourself. It won’t help if Jim thinks this is just me pushing some kind of touchy-feely agenda here. Tell him what you’ve told me this morning, how vulnerable you feel, and see if he won’t meet you halfway. I could meet with you both on Thursday late afternoon, or we can try to find another time that works better. In the meantime, here’s a card for a good pastoral counselor who specializes in couples. Maybe you and Jim will just want to go ahead and start counseling directly.”

			Elaine retrieves her cane and stands, wobbling slightly as the pain shoots up her left leg. “Thanks, Terence,” she says. Regaining her balance, Elaine stands taller and walks to the door. “I’ll talk with Jim about all this. I do feel a little more hopeful—for both Jim and me.”





Chris


			Chris put on a kettle for tea and ushered Malik into the chapel office, feeling nervous but hoping to convey a sense of confidence they didn’t really feel. After taking their seats, Chris started asking questions to gain more clarity about what happened.

			“So, Malik, can you tell me what happened in your own words?”

			Malik looked down at the floor, and said softly, “I was just leaving the football game. I stopped to pick up a couple of empty beer cans, you know, to help clean up, and three students I recognized from a class where I had made a comment about the prevalence of Islamophobia just jumped on me. They were beating me up, calling me names like ‘Abdul’ and ‘rag head,’ and swearing at me.” Looking up, he said, “The girl was the worst. She was screaming in my ear, ‘Go home, we don’t want terrorists here!’”

			Still feeling very anxious and trying to remember their good active listening skills, Chris said, “That must have been scary for you!”

			Malik looked down again. “Yes.” He lapsed into silence.

			Chris quickly said, “Your English is very good, Malik.” Malik made no response. “I’m sorry,” Chris continued, “but I have to ask this: Did you say or do anything at all to provoke this?”

			Malik looked at Chris, astonished and appalled. “No!” he said firmly.

			“OK, sorry, I just had to ask. Once the college provost releases a statement to the campus, there will be a lot of questions, and I needed to know how to answer them. The college will be conducting its own disciplinary procedure, but since what happened, assuming we know the whole story, is probably a hate crime, it has to be handled first by the outside authorities. Malik, will you press charges?”

			Malik looked up again. “I don’t really have a choice. The campus police reported it already as an assault. It’s up to the local police right now.”

			“Is that what you want?” Chris asked, thinking perhaps it would be better for Malik if the whole situation were just dealt with quietly by the college, and the student body wouldn’t get into an uproar around the incident. “Or maybe would you even prefer to transfer to another school and just get away from it all? Have you talked to your parents yet?”

			Malik sighed. So many questions. “I call my parents every Sunday night, but I’m not telling them about this. Normally I would consult them about any big incident,72 but right now I don’t want them to worry. They live in Pakistan, and it would be very difficult for them to come here. And no, I don’t want to transfer! It took hard work to get into this college, and to qualify as premed. My parents are proud that I’m here, and I don’t want them to be upset.” Taking a more assertive tone, he added, “And why should I be the one to have to leave the school?”

			Malik began to feel like a teacher with a very dull student. “This is not the first time I have faced prejudice, and I know it won’t be the last.”

			“Do you hate them, Malik?” Chris asked, feeling more and more out of their depth.

			“I do not hate them. But yes, I will testify.”

			Knowing the college would like to keep the incident as low-key as possible, Chris suggested, “Do you think it would help if I went and talked with those students and their parents, as the college chaplain? Maybe if I talked with them, they would be able to promise never to do such a thing again, and they could at least finish their degree?”

			Malik paused, then said, “Their parents will try to say it was just college drinking or rowdiness getting out of hand, and these students’ futures shouldn’t be destroyed because of one instance of bad judgment. That’s happened in plenty of other situations. But the racism and bigotry won’t stop if the whole thing gets minimized. I want to be able to tell my side of what happened, and personally, I believe those students should be expelled. This was not a minor situation. I’m still in a lot of pain.” Then he added, “Most of all, I don’t want other students to be attacked like I was. Muslims believe we should fight for what is right, not out of anger but using reason.”

			Chris, not knowing what more to say, and feeling torn between their own outrage on behalf of Malik and suspicion that the college would just like this all to go away quietly, asked, “So would you call this a kind of good jihad?”

			Malik took a deep breath, and answered patiently, “I hear that question very often about jihad. The word ‘jihad’ means ‘struggle.’ It is the constant human struggle to do the right thing. Fighting someone to advance justice could be considered jihad—but that is the lesser jihad. The greater jihad is to always be mindful of God and to do the right thing.73 That is what I am trying to do now.”

			Chris, feeling chastened by Malik’s response, nodded and said, “I admire you! Is there anything else I can do for you? I think there is a Qur’an around here somewhere that we could read from.”

			Malik rose from his seat, saying, “Thank you, Chaplain, I think you’ve done enough,” and left Chris’s office. Chris knew they had failed him but had no idea what to do to repair this awkward encounter. Like Malik, Chris was still new to the campus and the town, and although they had begun researching spiritual care resources for non-Christians either within the college or beyond it, they did not yet have a good handle on local provisions for Muslim students. Chris was floundering.





Self-Care


			What if, rather than turning immediately to scramble through the research they had stored on their computer, Chris had taken a moment to take a deep breath, drink that cup of tea that neither of them had touched during the visit, and tap into their own emotions after Malik left their office? Chris was clearly thrown off balance by this encounter and had made one blunder after another in the meeting. Under perceived pressure from the provost, they had neglected to put Malik first, coming across more like an investigative reporter than a pastoral caregiver. They needed first to take a moment for self-care and grounding.

			As Chris replays the encounter in their head, they might be able to let go a bit of the pressure of their new role, which is making them brittle and unable to access their usually good pastoral skills. Chris can recognize that their sense of duty to the college and its reputation took mental precedence over their primary duty to care for the hurting young student. Chris’s discomfort after Malik left was a signal to take a pause and reflect, and, without resorting immediately to their research, just think about who, without breaking Malik’s confidentiality, might be able to provide Chris with some needed resources, and even some pastoral supervision. Chris knows there is a small Muslim student association on campus as well as an interfaith caucus, both of which meet in the chapel. Chris also knows there is a mosque in the small city a few miles away. Their imam came to campus once during orientation. Chris might have directed Malik to him for prayers and support. Maybe Malik has already reached out to him? Chris hadn’t thought to ask.

			Chris wants to reach out to Malik again and try to provide him with better support but needs to compose themself first. Chris goes into the chapel for a few moments of centering prayer. Then, returning to the office, they look up the phone number for the mosque and leave a message for the imam, asking him to call them back as soon as possible. They need help in connecting Malik with appropriate support, and—just as important—they need some consultation or supervision themself.





Classical Countertransference


			Feeling more settled in their appropriate student-centered role, Chris is now able to think more clearly about what internal conflicts might have been creating obstacles to being fully responsive to Malik. Chris now mentally reviews the conversation with the student, and cringes at their own insensitivity. Why had they not listened more than they talked, and why had they felt the need to break every silence by asking questions? From clinical pastoral training, Chris knows better. So why? Sitting quietly, Chris realizes how nervous they had felt throughout. They had been so preoccupied with saying the right things that they ended up being “too much in their head” as one supervisor used to remark. Chris had lost touch of the ability to feel with Malik, and to let the student’s story of victimization simply unfold without pressure. Where was this anxiety coming from? Chris consciously feels firmly committed to justice and diversity, and in seminary was increasingly confirmed in the desire as a white, nonbinary person to be actively anti-racist and open to multiple religious faiths. So why was meeting with this Muslim student so disconcerting?

			Chris’s thoughts wander back to their own upbringing. They recall an issue that came up often in hospital chaplaincy training—an overwhelming need always to do the right thing, and to obey authority. When under clinical supervision, this sometimes was even helpful because both Chris and the supervisor (the authority) had the same focus—the wellbeing of the patient. But Chris is not under clinical supervision anymore. The “patient” is the student, Malik, while the “supervisor” is the provost (and by extension the administration), and Chris’s automatic reaction is to be dutiful to the provost’s authority first. Chris wanted to be 100 percent prepared for whatever questions might come their way after the statement to the community, to defend the school, and assert its “zero tolerance” for harassment and violence. Chris realizes that sometimes the role of a chaplain is to stand apart a bit from their institution to advocate for the person who is suffering. Care and justice for Malik must be the first priority, which also means answering any questions that may arise with sensitivity and a view toward Malik’s confidentiality. As chaplain, Chris needs to follow the college’s institutional processes, and wait for a full investigation to be carried out according to the stated harassment policy. But it is within Chris’s role to be mindful of a student’s needs first. “Doing the right thing” does not mean trying to represent the institution as if it were Chris’s job to take over the investigation. The provost, Alice, simply wanted to bring them up to speed about what had happened, and facilitate Chris’s meeting in order to provide spiritual care for Malik.

			Chris now further recognizes a deeper dimension of countertransference resulting from their upbringing, in the form of deeply ingrained prejudices and unconscious bias.74 Chris grew up in an extreme fundamentalist Christian church—virtually a commune—not too far from Mountbrook College. Chris—baptized “Christopher”—was a sensitive child, bullied by other children from early on. Classmates taunted them with homophobic slurs and made fun of their preference for books and music over athletics. As a teen, Chris tried to fit in, becoming an excellent swimmer, and dating a few girls. But internally, Chris was increasingly sure that they were gay, and felt very uncomfortable trying to conform to the masculine identity assumed by their family and church. Their junior year in high school they tried one night at dinner to describe this to their parents. Their father threatened to send Chris to a conversion therapy camp,75 but their mother intervened, saying, “Oh, Bob, it’s just a phase. When he goes to Bible College, he’ll be OK.” The next day they all pretended the conversation had never happened.

			Chris’s one rebellion upon graduation was to refuse to go to the Christian school their parents had assumed he would attend. Their good grades and proficiency as a swimmer paid off with a scholarship to an excellent, large university in another state. There they found a supportive LGBTQ+ community, and, as well, a more liberal form of Christianity through the campus ministry. They had minimal contact with their family, who continued to treat Chris as the devout “Bible-believing” Christian and straight young man they wanted Chris to be. Every visit, every phone call, dragged Chris back into a regressive sinkhole of self-denial and pretense. Eventually, they confided their spiritual struggles to the college chaplain, who saw promise in Chris’s capacity for deep theological reflection, and suggested pursuing a master of divinity degree.

			Upon graduating from college with honors, Chris enrolled in a progressive nondenominational seminary known for both rigorous academics and challenging field work. There, they became immersed in a new world of progressive theology. About one-third of the students were not Christian—declaring themselves unaffiliated, or following other faith traditions including Buddhism, Judaism, Islam, and Wicca/earth spirituality. Professors and classmates worked hard together to resist racism, heterosexism, and ableism, and all students were required to take in-depth courses in interreligious engagement. Classes addressed postmodern and postcolonial theory as applied in theology and reflective praxis. One professor, who himself was both a Catholic priest and a vowed Buddhist, even affirmed the idea of multiple religious belonging and introduced the students to a growing body of literature on the subject.76 The chaplaincy concentration, to which Chris found themself drawn, had an especially diverse cohort of students.

			As Chris’s spiritual formation deepened, alongside joining a supportive LGBTQ+ campus community, Chris rejected the birth name “Christopher,” and felt new freedom in their emerging identity as trans/nonbinary. The LGBTQ+ caucus celebrated this coming out with a renaming ceremony. Around the same time, Chris also claimed a dual Christian/Humanist religious identity, and increasingly rejected (at least consciously) the coercive and stifling version of Christianity of their upbringing. They increasingly felt fed intellectually by the freedom from dogma they found as a Humanist, while retaining their core love for Jesus and his teachings. Chris found a warm welcome for their gifts in both their chaplaincy internship and residency, eventually being certified as an Interfaith Chaplain.

			Coming to serve as chaplain at Mountbrook has brought Chris close to home again, geographically, and while they still do not visit their parents often, they are back in closer touch. Mountbrook, while not an evangelical school, has a long history as a college founded by a Protestant denomination in the nineteenth century, and in spite of good intentions about being more interfaith, the chaplaincy office still reflects mostly symbols, liturgical forms, and religious language of Christianity. Hiring Chris was in fact an effort to widen the college’s welcome to students of other faiths and none. But the chaplaincy is an office of one person, and activities for non-Christian students are carried out mostly by student groups and local congregations.

			Chris realizes, thinking about Malik, that being in the insular town of Easton, near their parents once again, and in a religious job where Christianity was still the norm, they have unconsciously fallen back into some of the more harmful messages they had received as a child. Muslims, in particular, were feared and despised as terrorists who were out to destroy Christianity and take over the United States and the world. Chris was eleven at the time of the terrorist attacks on September 11, 2001, and from that time on, the pastor of their church became more and more strident about the necessity to ban Muslims from the country, and to restore the United States as a Christian nation, governed according to a fundamentalist reading of biblical law.77

			Chris had thought they had thoroughly repudiated these ideas while in college and seminary. But when they walked into the provost’s office and were confronted with the sight of Malik, bandaged and beaten, Chris’s first reaction, unbidden and immediately suppressed, had not been, “What violence has happened to this student?” but, “What violence has this Muslim student started?” Images of 9/11 flashed through Chris’s mind, as well as dire warnings from their childhood about Islamist terrorists. Upon reflection, Chris now realizes that unconsciously they were afraid of Malik. Chris now feels deeply ashamed of this realization but remembers from anti-racism workshops in seminary that white shame and guilt, without actively working for change, do not help anyone.78 He resolves to do better, and hopes for a chance to meet up with Malik again in a more conscious and empathic way.





The Social Countertransference


			Up to this point in this book, classical countertransference has been described as a largely interior phenomenon in reaction to someone who comes to us for support. This is, of course, where countertransference is felt and makes its appearance through unempathic words and enactments. Transference and countertransference, as Freud first described, are products of the helpee’s and helper’s unresolved inner conflicts and childhood wounds. But transference and countertransference are not solely products of our immediate parental and familial upbringing. Beginning in earliest childhood, as noted in chapter 3, we absorb the attitudes, beliefs, and biases that characterize not only our familial environment, but also our societal, cultural, economic, and political contexts. We unconsciously absorb the wider context of our upbringing like a sponge. The child’s development of language reinforces these beliefs at a more conscious level, but studies show that even at the earliest preverbal stage, children are highly sensitive to their parents’ and other significant adults’ affective life. They internalize not only the individual intrapsychic anxieties of their parents, but also a thick layer of culturally determined fears, aversions, and preferences long before they have any developmental capacity to evaluate them.79

			While countertransference does reflect some of the earliest experiences and feelings absorbed from the familial environment, it also includes subliminal societal and cultural messages that are internalized and kept alive in one’s unconscious landscape of internal objects, or inner self states. These function unconsciously both as ways of remaining attached to our earliest caregivers in whom we depended literally for survival as small children, and as unconscious beliefs about the relative safety or danger of the outside world and what it takes to survive. Contemporary psychoanalytic theory has increasingly come to recognize the impact of context, beyond the immediate family, on the formation of each individual’s internal worlds. Cultural and societal context, just like parental expectations, molds the child’s desires, anxieties, and conflicts, and these may remain buried in the unconscious, only to emerge later as little comprehended enactments in both personal and public life.





Preliminary Pastoral Assessment


			Having gained clarity about their unconscious pulls toward authority and disavowed unconscious racism and Islamophobia, Chris is able to see Malik’s needs more clearly. Chris’s first mistake was to ask him to go across campus to meet at the chapel office. A less isolated place, and moreover, less saturated with Christian symbols, would have put Malik more at ease—or at least less obviously feeling like a vulnerable “Other.” What if Chris had simply asked Alice to use the small conference room next door to her office? Second, and of key importance, Malik needed someone to really listen to him—not just to gather facts, but to hear and validate Malik’s feelings from the time of the assault until now. Malik does not need someone to put words in his mouth, to make assumptions, or to pepper him with questions. And third, Malik may need an advocate in the institutional system. Fortunately, Desmond already stood by Malik as a witness and an ally. But it can also be helpful, and is often within the role of chaplains in a variety of institutional settings, to bring the immediate needs of the patient, or student—especially a survivor of an assault!—to the attention of administrators whose duty is also to follow policies put in place for the protection of all students. There is nothing to suggest up to this point that the provost has been anything other than sensitive to Malik’s trauma. Nevertheless, a chaplain can continue to bring the needs of one who is suffering to the forefront, strengthening lines of relationship between those in authority and those seeking help, ultimately best serving everyone in the system.





Intersubjective Countertransference and Further Pastoral Assessment


			Now that Chris has come to recognize how their classical countertransference was getting in the way of empathically listening to Malik, with greater clarity about Malik’s pastoral needs, Chris is in a much better position to provide appropriate, “trauma informed” care. Yet Chris has still not fully dealt with the emotion that arose as they first entered the situation, and which persisted in Chris’s own affective state as they interviewed Malik—a deep and pervasive feeling of anxiety. Yes, being nervous could be related to their classical countertransference about pleasing those in authority, and the prickle of fear they felt regarding Malik no doubt related to their own unconscious bias and Islamophobia. However, Chris has felt such emotions in other situations and had not experienced such high anxiety. Was there something more happening beyond their own intrapsychic conflicts that heightened the fear? Chris asks themself the question they learned in their training about intersubjective relationship: “What might my own feelings and reactions tell me about what the other person is experiencing that they haven’t yet put into words? What might my over-the-top anxiety tell me about what Malik is feeling?”

			Once the question is asked, the answer comes to Chris readily: Of course, Malik is feeling anxious. He has just gone through a violent assault. In the immediate aftermath of such a trauma, shock and numbness may initially be the predominant presenting affective state. Fear, tears, or rage may come later, but at the moment, Malik likely is not ready to name all his feelings even to himself until he has more time to process what has happened. Chris’s fear is not necessarily only Chris’s own, then, but may also be a reflection of emotions that are being communicated intersubjectively, through the channel of unconscious relationship. Nevertheless, Chris is perhaps sensing—and directly experiencing—Malik’s unspoken anxiety, which is there underneath, hidden safely in other self-parts.

			The fear may also be multifaceted for Malik. In addition to the fear associated with the trauma, there may be a distrust and fear of an inexperienced white chaplain, whom he also views as a representative of the institution. Would Chris take Malik’s story of the assault seriously enough? Consciously, in relating to Chris, Malik turns to his faith (he’s talking with the chaplain, after all) and presents himself quite formally, as a devoted Muslim. The devout person of faith is a familiar and protective self-state within Malik’s psychological makeup, deeply rooted and true. This bedrock faith has often strengthened him and served as an anchor in times of discrimination and oppression.
