Chapter Three


			 1. African American spiritual, in This Far by Faith: An African American Resource for Worship (Minneapolis: Augsburg Fortress, 1999), hymn 66.



			 2. “Redlining” is a racist and economically discriminatory practice, now illegal, in which banks and retail stores draw a “red line” around neighborhoods, usually in the inner city, in which they refuse to locate branches or franchises, deny loans to residents, or provide lower quality services and goods. The term originated in the 1930s in mortgage banking, when, under the New Deal, the Home Owners Loan Corporation (HOLC) devised a four-color rating system to determine a neighborhood’s level of eligibility for mortgage loans. “Part of the theory in the ’30s and ’40s was that race and prosperity were interconnected. A green neighborhood meant low-risk, while blue and yellow neighborhoods were more risky. Red neighborhoods were considered the riskiest and were to be avoided. As a result of that coding, less than 20 percent of FHA and VHA loans were made to people of color in the 1930s and 1940s.” Gary Warth, “Research Project Looking at Red-lining,” The Californian/North County Times, May 5, 2002, citing researcher Richard Marciano at the University of San Diego; Keenaga-Yamatta Taylor, Race for Profit: How Banks and the Real Estate Industry Undermined Black Home Ownership (Chapel Hill, NC: University of North Carolina Press, 2021). The trend spread to supermarket chains in the 1960s, along with the building of megastores in the suburbs, resulting in a serious lack of availability of affordable, fresh food in the inner cities (Bill Turque, Deborah Rosenberg, and Todd Barrett, “Where the Food Isn’t,” Newsweek, February 24, 1992, https://www.newsweek.com/where-food-isnt-200642) and availability of health care (Maria Godoy, “In U.S. Cities, the Health Effects of Past Housing Discrimination Are Plain to See,” NPR, November 19, 2020, https://www.npr.org/sections/health-shots/2020/11/19/911909187/in-u-s-cities-the-health-effects-of-past-housing-discrimination-are-plain-to-see.)



			 3. Emilie Townes, Breaking the Fine Rain of Death: African American Health Issues and a Womanist Ethic of Care (New York: Continuum, 1998). This health disparity became even more evidence during the COVID-19 pandemic in 2020 and beyond, e.g., Juanita Chinn, Iman Martin, and Nicole Redmond, “Health Equity among Black Women in the United States,” Journal of Women’s Health 30, no. 2 (2021): 212–219, https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC8020496/.



			 4. L. K. Graham, “From Relational Humanness to Relational Justice,” 220–234.



			 5. Monica McGoldrick, Nydia A. Garcia Preto, and Betty Carter, The Expanding Family Life Cycle: Individual and Social Perspectives, 5th ed. (Boston: Pearson, 2016), inside front cover and 16.



			 6. I am grateful for consultations from Rev. Carla Harris, Rev. Dr. Eloise Scott, and Rev. Margaret Tyson, whose rich feedback has helped to bring Linda to voice in relation to the experience of African American women ministers. As a white author of cases with multiple cultural and class dimensions throughout this book, I am aware that I stand on “holy ground,” and I approach the task of writing cases with a deep sense of humility and circumspection. I do so out of a commitment to including social and cultural context in our pastoral theological casework, a dimension that has been too long neglected. I have engaged in “vicarious introspection” to the extent that is possible, but true to my own constructivist position, I am aware that one can never fully represent the experiences of another, particularly one outside one’s own social location. Case studies are finally meant to tell the story of individuals, with their own unique, particular intrapsychic dynamics and social locations. Neither Linda, Gary, Terence, Chris nor Sara is intended to be paradigmatic of an entire cultural, ethnic, or class group. I am deeply grateful for the honest, illuminating feedback of readers from diverse backgrounds. Any errors or infelicities of expression are mine alone.



			 7. Thandeka, in Learning to Be White: Money, Race, and God in America (New York: Continuum, 2000), 3, describes an exercise called the Race Game: “The Race Game, as my luncheon partner very quickly discovered, had only one rule. For the next seven days, she must use the ascriptive term white whenever she mentioned the name of one of her Euro-American cohorts. She must say, for instance, ‘my white husband, Phil,’ or ‘my white friend Julie,’ or ‘my lovely white child Jackie.’ . . . I guaranteed her that if she did this for a week and then met me for lunch, I could answer her question [what it felt like to be black] using terms she would understand. We never had lunch together again. Apparently my suggestion had made her uncomfortable. African Americans have learned to use a racial language to describe themselves and others. Euro-Americans also have learned a pervasive racial language. But in their racial lexicon, their own racial group becomes the great unsaid [also citing labor historian David Roediger, Towards the Abolition of Whiteness (London: Verso, 1994), 12]. I wanted my luncheon partner to give voice to her whiteness as the racial unsaid in her life.”



			 8. Cf., Jacques Derrida, Positions, trans. Alan Bass (Chicago: University of Chicago Press, 1985), 39. For Derrida, the “play of differences” and his term différance refer to the distance between signifiers and what they signify, in both time and space, erasing all essentialisms. I am using this term to mean both this slipperiness of verbal meaning and the phenomenologically felt differences among various groups as they are socially and culturally constructed through gender, race, class, and so forth.



			 9. Emmanuel Lartey, In Living Color. This distinction was first made by anthropologist Florence Kluckhohn, “Dominant and Variant Value Orientations,” in Personality in Nature, Society, and Culture, ed. Clyde Kluckholn, Henry Alexander Murray, and D. M. Schneider (New York: Knopf, 1953), 342–357. For more on Kluckhohn’s value orientations approach to culture, see Florence Kluckhohn and Frederick L. Strodtbeck, Variations in Value Orientations (Evanston, IL: Row Peterson, 1961). See also Orlo Strunk Jr., “Multicultural Counseling and the Systemic Notion: Implication for Pastoral Psychotherapy,” in Therapeutic Practice in a Cross-Cultural World: Theological, Psychological, and Ethical Issues, ed. Carole Bohn (Decatur, GA: Journal of Pastoral Care Publications, 1995), 1–16.



			 10. This method, like many others proposed by practical theologians, follows a cycle of practice-reflection-practice first articulated in the pastoral theology field by Don Browning, A Fundamental Practical Theology, esp. pp. 1–9. For a concise summary of a number of practical theological practice-reflection-practice models, see P. and M. Cooper-White, Exploring Practices of Ministry (Minneapolis: Fortress, 2014), esp. pp. 11–27; see also Bonnie Miller-McLemore, “Introduction: The Contributions of Practical Theology,” in The Wiley Blackwell Companion to Practical Theology, ed. Miller-McLemore (Malden, MA: Wiley, 2014), 1–4.



			 11. Sigmund Freud, “Wild Analysis” (1910), SE 11: 219–228. Freud coined the term “wild analysis” to refer to analysis that is rushed into without thoughtful consideration, making a “wrong surmise” (p. 226).



			 12. Marsha Foster Boyd stresses the key role of listening in pastoral care with African American women in terms of “the privilege one is afforded to listen as another tells her or his story. A theology of Womanist Care, then, seeks to ensure that African American women are ‘listened into life.’” Boyd, “Womanist Care: Some Reflections on the Pastoral Care and the Transformation of African American Women,” in Embracing the Spirit: Womanist Perspectives on Hope, Salvation, and Transformation, ed. Emilie M. Townes (Maryknoll, NY: Orbis, 1997), 200.



			 13. Because this book focuses on countertransference and use of the self, the sample assessments given here are not formal, extensive diagnostic reports. Cooper-White, Many Voices, esp. 96–102; Dagmar Grefe and Pamela McCarroll with Bilal Ansari, “Meaning Making in Chaplaincy Practice: Presence, Assessment, and Interventions,” in Wendy Cadge and Shelly Rambo, eds., Chaplaincy and Spiritual Care in the Twenty-First Century (Chapel Hill, NC: University of North Carolina Press, 2022), 66–89; and an interdisciplinary model, Christina Puchalski, “The FICA Spiritual History Tool,” online at https://gwish.org/sites/g/files/zaskib1011/files/2022-06/FICA-Tool-PDF-ADA.pdf.



			 			14. “Palliative care (derived from the Latin root palliare, or ‘to cloak’) is an interdisciplinary medical caregiving approach aimed at optimizing quality of life. nd mitigating suffering among people with serious, complex, and often terminal. llnesses.” Online at https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palliative_care, citing Timothy Quill, Vyjeyanthi Periyakoil, Erin Denny-Koelsch, Patrick White, and Donna Zhukovsky, Primer of Palliative Care, 7th ed. (Chicago, IL: American Association of Hospice and Palliative Medicine, 2019).



			 15. Analogy from Patrick Casement, Listening to the Patient (New York: Guilford, 1985), 38–41.



			 16. The term “free association” was first used by Sigmund Freud as early as the 1890s; see Josef Breuer and Sigmund Freud, “Studies on Hysteria” (1893), in SE 2: 303–305. The term was defined in the technique papers of 1912–1914; e.g., Freud, “Dynamics of the Transference,” SE 12: 97–108; Freud, “Recommendations to Physicians Practicing Psychoanalysis,” SE 12: 111–120; and Freud, “On Beginning the Therapy,” SE 12: 121–144). Free association was the “fundamental rule” of psychoanalysis, in which patients were exhorted to try to say everything that came to mind. I use the term more broadly, as it is commonly used in contemporary psychoanalysis, to mean noticing everything that comes to mind: thoughts, feelings, images, ideas, music, bodily sensations, fantasies, or anything else that floats into consciousness.



			 17. A number of methods and resources for theological reflection have been published, e.g., David Tracy, The Analogical Imagination: Christian Theology and the Culture of Pluralism (New York: Crossroad, 1981); Theodore W. Jennings Jr., “Pastoral Theological Methodology,” in Dictionary of Pastoral Care and Counseling, ed. Rodney Hunter (Nashville, TN: Abingdon, 1990), 862–864; Patricia Killen and John DeBeer, The Art of Theological Reflection (New York: Crossroad, 1994); James D. Whitehead and Evelyn Eaton Whitehead, Method in Ministry: Theological Reflection and Christian Ministry (Kansas City: Sheed and Ward, 1995); Howard Stone and James Duke, How to Think Theologically (Minneapolis: Fortress, 1996); Robert Kinast, Let Ministry Teach: A Guide to Theological Reflection (Collegeville, MN: Liturgical Press, 1996); Making Faith-Sense: Theological Reflection in Everyday Life (Collegeville, MN: Liturgical Press, 1999); Kinast, What Are They Saying about Theological Reflection? (New York: Paulist, 2000); and Donald Capps and Gene Fowler, The Pastoral Care Case: Learning about Care in Congregations (St. Louis, MO: Chalice, 2001); Elaine Graham, Theological Reflection: Methods, 2nd ed. (London: SCM, 2019); Victor Gabriel and Duane Bidwell, “Leading and Facilitating Spiritual Reflection,” in Cadge and Rambo, eds, Chaplaincy and Spiritual Care in the Twenty-first Century, 90–109. For a specifically feminist methodology, see Doehring, “A Method of Feminist Pastoral Theology,” in Miller-McLemore and Austern, eds., Feminist and Womanist Pastoral Theology, 95–111. Jeffrey Mahan, Barbara Troxell, and Carol Allen provide a method for group reflection in Shared Wisdom: A Guide to Case Study Reflection in Ministry (Nashville, TN: Abingdon, 1993). A method for interreligious theological reflection may be found in Edward Foley, Theological Reflection across Religious Traditions: The Turn to Reflective Believing (Lanham, MD: Rowman and Littlefield, 2005).

Chapter Four


			 1. Thanks to chaplains Rev. Dr. Florence Gelo and Rev. Dr. Charles Lindholm for their consultations about Gary’s story.



			 			2. Such interdisciplinary collaboration is not to be assumed in all hospital settings, of course. For a discussion of the role of chaplaincy in patient care and the need for increased awareness of a chaplain’s role among medical, nursing, and other hospital staff, see, e.g., Larry VandeCreek and Jerry Royer, “Education for Interdisciplinary Teamwork,” Journal of Pastoral Care 29 (1975): 176–184; VandeCreek, The Chaplain-Physician Relationship (New York: Haworth, 1991); VandeCreek and Laurel Burton, “Professional Chaplaincy: Its Role and Importance in Health Care,” Journal of Pastoral Care 55, no. 1 (2001): 81–97; Renske Kruizinga, Carmen Schumann, Tina Glasner, and Gary Jacobs, “Enhancing the Integration of Chaplains within the Healthcare Team A Qualitative Analysis of a Survey Study among Healthcare Chaplains,” Integrated Healthcare Journal 4, no. 1 (2023): e000138, DOI: 10.1136/ihj-2022-000138; and Cadge and Rambo, eds., Chaplaincy and Spiritual Care in the Twenty-first Century, esp. Part 4: “Organizational Competencies,” 193–257.



			 3. Robert Wicks, “Burnout,” in Essentials for Chaplains, ed. Sharon Cheston and Robert Wicks (New York: Paulist, 1993), 2–3. On reflective practices in working life, see also Parker Palmer, The Active Life: A Spirituality of Work and Creativity (San Francisco: Jossey-Bass, 1999); and Let Your Life Speak: Listening for the Voice of Vocation (San Francisco: Jossey-Bass, 1999).



			 4. Owen Renik, “Countertransference, Enactment and the Psychoanalytic Process,” in Structure and Psychic Change, ed. Mardi Jon Horowitz, Otto Kernberg, and Edward Weinshel (Madison, CT: International Universities Press, 1993), 135–158.



			 5. See, e.g., Bruce Epperly, A Center in the Cyclone: Twenty-first Century Clergy Self-Care (Lanham, MD: Rowman and Littlefield, 2014); Donald R. Hands and Wayne L. Fehr, Spiritual Wholeness for Clergy (Washington, DC: Alban Institute, 1993); Kirk Byron Jones, Fulfilled: Living and Leading with Unusual Wisdom, Peace, and Joy (Nashville, TN: Abingdon, 2013); Rochelle Melander and Harold Eppley, The Spiritual Leader’s Guide to Self-Care (Bethesda, MD: Alban Institute, 2002); Roy Oswald, Clergy Self-Care: Finding a Balance for Effective Ministry (Washington, DC: Alban Institute, 1991); Oswald, How to Build a Support System for Your Ministry (Washington, DC: Alban Institute, 1991); Robert Wicks, Christian Introspection: Self-Ministry through Self-Understanding (New York: Crossroad, 1983). See also A. Richard Bullock and Richard Bruehshoff, Clergy Renewal: The Alban Guide to Sabbatical Planning (Lanham, MD: Rowman and Littlefield, 2000); Robert Saler, Planning Sabbaticals: A Guide for Congregations and their Pastors (St. Louis, MO: Chalice, 2022).



			 6. My own social location is both like and unlike that of the composite cases in this book. As a woman born in 1955 and educated in the nontraditional professions of ministry and psychoanalytic therapy, I have experienced much of what has been written about gender discrimination and both the oppression and the empowerment of women through movements for social change in the radical decades of the 1960s and 1970s. My class background presents a mixed-class picture. The home in which I grew up was much like Tex Sample’s “respectables” (right down to the chain-link fence!) in Blue Collar Ministry: Facing Economic and Social Realities of Working People (Valley Forge, PA: Judson, 1984), 74. My family was settled, “church-going, tee-totaling, politically and socially conservative” as described by Joseph T. Howell, Hard Living on Clay Street (New York: Doubleday/Anchor, 1973), 6, cited in Sample, Blue Collar Ministry, 73. The town in which I lived was a bedroom community both for the small industrial city of Lynn, Massachusetts, and for the more cosmopolitan but subjectively distant city of Boston. Lynn was dominated by two General Electric plants, where my great-great-uncle, great-uncle, and father all worked, moving up over the decades from bookkeeping and accounting to upper middle management, while my grandfather worked a variety of blue-collar jobs, including Brinks truck driver, private security police, mechanic, and service station owner. Both my father and grandfather were veterans, the former during the Korean occupation, and the latter in the cavalry ride against Pancho Villa in 1917 and as a fighter pilot in World War I. My parents and grandparents were deeply involved in both the American Legion and the Masons, where my father continues to volunteer indefatigably in his retirement. The population of my hometown was all white, with strong religious and white-ethnic enclaves, most predominantly Italian Catholics, white Anglo-Saxon Protestants (our family’s religious and ethnic identity), and a large population of Conservative and Reform Jews. There were many first- and second-generation Holocaust survivors. By virtue of my father’s rising economic and social status and my own university and Ivy League graduate education and academic profession, I understand the experience of Gary’s trajectory into the “professional class.” Like many academics and therapists, my present-day life best fits a blend of Paul Fussell’s upper-middle and “X” classes, in Class: A Guide through the American Status System (New York: Touchstone, 1992), 179–188, defined more by education and the conscious adoption of certain social values than by income.



			 7. Fussell, Class, 87–94, 194. See also Meck Groot, “On Being Working-Class Educated,” in “Hearing the Voices of Poor and Working-Class Women,” special issue, Journal of Women and Religion 12 (1993): 11–15.



			 8. Leave it to Beaver was an American sitcom from 1957 to 1963 often used as shorthand for an idealized white middle-class family life.



			 9. Tex Sample describes the “blue-collar respectable” group, for whom dignity, discipline, hard work, and a home that symbolizes security and decency are prevailing values, in Blue Collar Ministry, 71–83. For further discussions of working-class life in America, see Karen Bloomquist, The Dream Betrayed: Religious Challenges of the Working Class (Minneapolis: Fortress, 1990); Herbert Gutman, Power and Culture: Essays on the American Working Class, ed. Ira Berlin (New York: Pantheon, 1987); John Raines and Donna Day-Lower, Modern Work and Human Meaning (Philadelphia, PA: Westminster John Knox, 1986); Lillian Breslow Rubin, Worlds of Pain: Life in the Working-Class Family (New York: Basic Books, 1976), 128; Rubin, Families on the Fault Line: America’s Working Class Speaks about the Family, the Economy, Race and Ethnicity (New York: HarperCollins, 1994); Richard Sennett and Jonathan Cobb, The Hidden Injuries of Class (New York: Knopf, 1972); Roy Theriault, Unmasking the American Working Class (New York: New Press, 2003); and Erik Olin Wright, “American Class Structure,” American Sociological Review 47 (1982): 709–726; Noam Chomsky, Working Class History (New York: PM Press, 2020); David Leonhardt and Stuart Thompson, “How Working Class Life is Killing Americans,” New York Times, March 6, 2020, online at https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2020/03/06/opinion/working-class-death-rate.html.



			 10. Sample, Blue Collar Ministry, documents the aspirations of “respectable” parents for their children to go to college. For a more nuanced view of working-class parents’ pride and ambivalence, see also Rubin, Worlds of Pain, 207–209.



			 11. Barbara Ehrenreich describes the “professional-managerial class” in Fear of Falling: The Inner Lift of the Middle Class (New York: Pantheon, 1989).



			 12. First-person account by bel hooks, “Crossing Class Boundaries,” in Where We Stand: Class Matters (London: Routledge, 2000), 143.



			 13. For a number of first-person essays on this liminal state from similar perspectives of academics from the working class, see C. L. Barney Dews and Carolyn Leste Law, eds., This Fine Place So Far from Home: Voices of Academics from the Working Class (Philadelphia, PA: Temple University Press, 1995). See also bel hooks, “Coming to Class Consciousness,” in Where We Stand, 24–37; and “Hearing the Voices of Poor and Working-Class Women,” special issue, Journal of Women and Religion 12 (1993): 1–73.



			 14. Barbara Ehrenreich documents how, in spite of minuscule increases in the minimum wage, the rising cost of housing in particular has exacerbated the divide between rich and poor, and created a growing but largely invisible subclass of working poor. Wage gains have not kept pace with inflation, and the poorest workers “have made the least progress back to their 1973 wage levels.” Nickel and Dimed: On (Not) Getting By in America (New York: Metropolitan Books/Henry Holt, 2001), 203. Further, “the shift from a blue-collar industrial economy to a service and information/technology economy will eventually dislocate 15 to 45 million workers in urban America.” Judith Orr, “Hard Work, Hard Lovin’, Hard Times, Hardly Worth It,” in The Care of Men, ed. Christie Cozad Neuger and James Newton Poling (Nashville, TN: Abingdon, 1997), 77, citing Richard Gillett, “Church Role Vital for Justice in New Workplaces,” The Witness 69, no. 2 (1986): 12–15.



			 15. Pertti Alasuutari explains drinking among working-class men as both an act of freedom from social constraints at work and at home, and a form of self-­medication to “numb the pain of not feeling man enough,” in “Alcoholism in Its Cultural Context: The Case of Blue Collar Men,” Contemporary Drug Problems 12 (winter 1986): 641–686, cited in Orr, “Hard Work, Hard Lovin’,” 95–96. Alasuutari also notes that the pattern of binge drinking is more prevalent among blue-collar men, while white-collar drinking tends to be more continual (p. 674). On binge drinking, see also Sample, Blue Collar Ministry. Sample identifies binge drinking in particular with “blue collar hard living.” Orr states, “Drinking is especially common among hard-living men, but is not uncommon among others as well,” p. 80, citing Ben Hamper, Rivethead· Tales from the Assembly Line (New York: Warner, 1986), 55.



			 16. Sonia Waters, Addiction and Pastoral Care (Grand Rapids, MI: Eerdmans, 2019), re: binge- withdrawal-craving, see esp. 39–42.



			 17. Orr, “Hard Work, Hard Lovin’,” 74, documents how the simplistic stereotype of the working-class male as “drunken, overweight, hedonistic, licentious, violent, loud, boyish, inarticulate, boring, and stupid” persists and is reinforced by media portrayals such as sitcom characters Archie Bunker and Al Bundy—in spite of the diversity of working-class experiences, lifestyles, self-perceptions, and levels of employment and wage.



			 18. “The six-pack of beer is merely the working-class version of the idle-class multiple martini lunch.” Orr, “Hard Work, Hard Lovin’,” 80.



			 19. Pierre Bourdieu, Language and Symbolic Power (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 1991), describes the domestication of language and body as one moves up the socioeconomic scale, the denial of social and sexual identity of working-class men, and the blunting of expression of appetites, feelings, and outspokenness. Cited in Orr, “Hard Work, Hard Lovin’,” 240.



			 20. A commonly used recent assessment tool, designed by doctors to determine whether a referral for further assessment and treatment is in order, is the AUDIT-C questionnaire with three questions addressing frequency and amount of drinking (https://cde.nida.nih.gov/instrument/f229c68a-67ce-9a58-e040-bb89ad432be4). An older tool, still in use for a quick preliminary assessment, the “CAGE” considers the “C” criterion—trying to cut back or control drinking—as a first sign of possible addiction (https://www.hopkinsmedicine.org/-/media/johns-hopkins-health-plans/documents/all_plans/cage-substance-screening-tool.pdf). “Controlled drinking” was a controversial alternative to 12-step programs, especially in the 1990s, but note that moderating drinking may be a successful strategy for those who do not fit criteria for physiological addiction—Cyndi Turner, “Controlled Drinking after 25 Years: How Important Was the Great Debate?” Addiction, 90 (1995): 1149–1150; see also Saladin, Michael E. and Elizabeth J. Santa Ana, “Controlled Drinking: More than Just a Controversy,” Current Opinion in Psychiatry, 17, no. 3 (2004): 175–187. The DSM-5-TR states four criteria for “Substance Use Disorder”: “impaired control, social problems, risky use, and pharmacological criteria” (physiological addiction and uncontrollable craving), Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders, 5th ed., text revision (hereafter: DSM-5-TR) (Washington, DC: American Psychiatric Association, 2022). Environment and reasons for drinking are also always considerations.



			 21. For detailed guidance in drawing a genogram, see Monica McGoldrick, Randy Gerson, and Sylvan Shellenberger, Genograms: Assessment and Intervention, 4th ed. (New York: W. W. Norton, 2020).



			 22. If Carol had direct evidence of alcohol impairing Gary’s professional life or interfering with his relationships, a referral to a specialized alcohol treatment facility for assessment would be preferable to a general counseling referral.



			 23. Readers are encouraged to look up services for domestic violence in their own locales—online see “Consulting Services,” RAINN at https://rainn.org/consulting-services#hotline. 			 71. Clergy and spiritual caregivers should be alert to signs of clinical depression and suicidal ideation. If any such symptoms are present or if there is any doubt about the possibility of suicidal ideation, the individual should be referred immediately to a psychotherapist, psychologist, or psychiatrist or mental health facility for a professional clinical evaluation, and in the interim the caregiver should work with the suicidal person to create a safety plan (see https://suicidesafetyplan.com/). If the disclosure is made by phone, refer the person to the national crisis hotline 988, or to text TALK to 741741. For more detailed information on suicide assessment and intervention, see the American Foundation for Suicide Prevention at https://afsp.org/. See especially “Brief Interventions for Managing Suicial Crises,” at https://afsp.org/brief-interventions-for-managing-suicidal-crises/#crisis-response-planning-(crp).

			 74. “Unconscious bias” can be defined as: “Snap judgements we make about people and situations based upon years of subconscious socialization. . . According to Mahzarin Banaji, the human brain is hard-wired to make quick decisions that draw on a variety of assumptions and experiences without us even knowing it is doing so, meaning that our unconscious predispositions can influence our decision making. Implicit biases are not deliberately or consciously created, but rather ‘they are products of our brain’s definition of normal, acceptable or positive, and they are shaped by many factors—from past experiences to our cultural environment to the influence of our social community and media around us’. . . Explicit bias [refers to] attitudes and beliefs, we have that we are fully aware of based on what’s being perceived. Explicit biases are usually directed toward a group of people. Unconscious [or] implicit bias [refers to] unintended, subtle, and subconscious associations learned through past experiences. [Such thoughts] happen to all of us, that we are unaware of on a conscious level.”—Harvard School of Public Health, Office of Diversity and Inclusion, https://www.hsph.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/sites/2597/2022/06/Types-of-Bias-Ways-to-Manage-Bias_HANDOUT-1.pdf. For more detail, see Pragya Agarwal, Sway: Unravelling Unconscious Bias (London: Bloomsbury, 2021); Mahzarin R. Banaji and Anthony G. Greenwald, Blind Spot: Hidden Biases of Good People (New York: Bantam, 2016).



			 75. “Conversion therapy,” also known as “reparative therapy” among other misleading labels, is a discredited pseudo-therapeutic practice designed to “convert” LGBTQ+ persons to a heterosexual identity and sexuality, often related to conservative Christian groups. The American Psychological Association removed the diagnosis “gender identity disorder” from the DSM-5-TR, and as of this writing, the practice is banned in nineteen US states and globally in at least thirteen countries. For a concise summary, see https://www.webmd.com/sex-relationships/what-is-conversion-therapy.

Greider, Claremont Studies in Interreligious Dialogue, (Claremont, CA: Claremont, 2019).

			 80. Thanks to Aleksandr Soran for this wording, personal communication, January 24, 2024. In Pakistan in particular, while same-sex acts have been prohibited by law since 1860 (under British rule), and there is a complex web of anti-LGBTQ+ “moral and public order provisions in the Penal Code” (“LGBT Rights in Pakistan,” Wikipedia, https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Pakistan), there are still social spaces for more openness, especially among younger people. LGBTQ+ movements for equality are growing, for example, the Indigenous Khawaja Sira/Hijra/trans community of South Asia. (personal communication, Arifa Khandwala, January 20, 2024; Muaz Chaudhry, January 20, 2024). “The Indo-Pakistani region has a rich history of acceptance of the trans community prior to being colonized.” (Chaudhry) “Trans persons (or the Hindustani word hijra, and in Pakistan, also by the Urdu word khawaja sira) ([n.a., “Hijra (South Asia),” wikipedia.com, https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hijra_(South_Asia)#cite_note-25). ere viewed as a third gender “during the time of the Prophet Muhammad [when] there was a rich history of sexual diversity. . .” (Soran, citing Khaled El-Rouayheb, Before Homosexuality in the Arab-Islamic World, Chicago: University of Chicago Press, 2005) “The present transphobia in Pakistan comes from adopting colonial attitudes, the permeation of Wahabism in Pakistan exported from Saudi Arabia, and adopting Western attitudes.” (Chaudhry) Trans persons are visible in Pakistani communities, frequently seen in public begging (personal communication, Afsheen A. Shamsi), but they face widespread bias and discrimination (Khandwal, Shamsi, Soran), causing them often to live at the margins of society.

Muslim communities value protest yet these actions are taken when the whole community decides, rather than an individualistic choice of each member.”—Personal communication, Alexandr Soran, January 24, 2024.

			 85. While “Muslim pastoral care” or “spiritual care” were not widely recognized as terms in many Muslim contexts before the twenty-first century, this has changed with the increasing numbers of Muslim chaplains in the United States and Europe, also prompted in part by the great need for spiritual care by Muslims who faced increasing persecution after 9/11, and more recently, the surge of Muslim migrants seeking asylum in Europe and the US. Muslim chaplaincy research and development of best practices is also being conducted, e.g., Hansjörg Schmidt and Amir Sheikhzadegan, “A Muslim Chaplaincy for Asylum Seekers? 			 86. In particularly remote locations or in contexts of natural disaster or war, spiritual caregivers, chaplains, or psychotherapists may not have the luxury of immediate consultation. However, even in such isolated circumstances, support may be sought through the internet (or even satellite phone). As an example, the Sigmund Freud Museum, Vienna, has been hosting a continuing series of facilitated support groups for psychotherapists in Ukraine ever since the Russian invasion in February 2022 (“Psychotherapy under Conditions of War,” available on YouTube at https://www.freud-museum.at/en/videos.) Humanitarian aid organizations may also provide support and resources for local caregivers. In some remote locations, such support may be planned in advance.

Chapter Five


			 1. For a comprehensive anthology representing best practices in chaplaincy, see Wendy Cadge and Shelly Rambo, eds., Chaplaincy and Spiritual Care in the Twenty-first Century; see also from a sociological perspective Cadge, Spiritual Care: The Everyday Work of Chaplains (Oxford, UK: Oxford University Press, 2022) and an earlier ethnographic study by Cadge, Paging God: Religion in the Halls of Medicine (Chicago: University of Chicago Press, 2013).



			 2. P. Cooper-White, “The Therapist’s Use of the Self: Countertransference in Pastoral Counseling and Clinical Social Work,” unpublished PhD dissertation, Institute for Clinical Social Work (Chicago), 2001, ProQuest Dissertation Publishing, #3023407; research findings summarized in P. Cooper-White, “The Use of the Self in Psychotherapy: A Comparative Study of Pastoral Counselors and Clinical Social Workers.”



			 3. In my study, only 5 percent of respondents, including both pastoral counselors and clinical social workers, self-identified as cognitive-behavioral, and less than 1 percent (one therapist) identified as humanistic-existentialist.



			 4. Adapted from Vincent D’Andrea and Peter Salovey, Peer Counseling: Skills and Perspectives (Palo Alto, CA: Science and Behavior Books, 1983), 63, citing Joseph Luft, Group Processes: An Introduction to Group Dynamics, 3rd ed. (New York: McGraw-Hill, 1984).



			 5. On gentle confrontation as proclamation in pastoral care, see Charles Taylor, The Skilled Pastor: Counseling as the Practice of Theology (Minneapolis: Fortress, 1991).



			 6. I have mainly chosen to use the terms “Latino” and “Latinx” interchangeably as umbrella terms for this highly diverse ethnic population, and I have retained “Latina” for the narrative of Sara and Miranda, because many women prefer to retain this gender-specific term. I do not use the term “Hispanic” (coined by President Richard Nixon and used by the US Census Bureau and many social scientists) except in quoting others’ research terminology because “only a fraction” of Latino/as actually are of Spanish descent, and the use of the Spanish language is a colonial, not an ethnic artifact. (Many people labeled “Latino” are also of Indigenous descent and still speak their native languages.) Any umbrella term for the original peoples of the American continent is problematic. As described by historian Marie Arana in Latinoland: A Portrait of America’s Largest and Least Understood Minority (New York: Simon and Schuster, 2024), xii-xiii, every term currently in use has been imposed by the dominant white US culture—“the academy, the government, the fad-mongers, the institutions.” (p. xiii) The term “Latinx” was developed in recognition of LGBTQ identities, to avoid the essentialist gender binary of “Latino” and “Latina.” Some Latina writers are uneasy, however, when their gender is subsumed under a generic term—as it was previously subsumed by the masculine term “Latino” to refer to both genders. LGBTQ activist Cherie Moraga told author Marie Arana, “I worked too hard for the ‘a’ in Latina to give it up! I refer to myself as Xicana.” (Arana, Latinoland, xiii) Ana María del Río-González argues that “Latinx” should be used to indicate multigender inclusivity to do LGBTQ persons due justice, but it should not be assumed as a new umbrella term that is seldom used by members of the ethnic group themselves (it is mostly current among younger people with higher formal education) and erases women’s self-identification as “Latina” (in “To Latinx or Not to Latinx: A Question of Gender Inclusivity versus Gender Neutrality,” American Journal of Public Health 111, no. 6 (2021): 1018–21).



			 7. I am indebted to several Puerto Rican professional church women, whose consultation helped me bring Linda authentically to voice: Sara Calderón, Rev. lvis LaRiviere-Mestre, and Melissa Ramirez Cooper, as well as consultation with Rev. Nelson Rivera.



			 8. For a discussion of the internalization of culture in counselors’ assumptions, see D. Y. F. Ho, “Internalized Culture, Culturocentrism, and Transcendence,” The Counseling Psychologist 23 (1995): 4–24. See also John Hinkle and Gregory Hinkle, “Surrendering the Self: Pastoral Counseling at the Limits of Culture and Psychotherapy,” in Therapeutic Practice in a Cross-Cultural World Theological, Psychological, and Ethical Issues, ed. Carole Bohn, JCPC Monograph no. 7 (Decatur, GA: Journal of Pastoral Care Publications, 1995), 77–98.



			 9. For a discussion of the history and challenges of Puerto Rican women and attendant pastoral issues, see Irma S. Corretjer-Nolla, “The Pastoral Care of the Puerto Rican Woman in the United States,” in Bridging Boundaries: The Pastoral Care of U.S. Hispanics, ed. Kenneth Davis and Yolanda Tarango (Scranton, PA: University of Scranton Press, 2000), 65–80. See also lvis LaRiviere-Mestre, “Working with Latinos: The Ministerial Implications of Reaching Out to Latino/Hispanic Families,” in Reaching the Latino Community: A Manual for Congregational Leaders, ed. Hector Carrasquilo and Giacomo Cassese (Chicago: Evangelical Lutheran Church in America, 2002), 45–52.



			 10. At the turn of the new millennium, male pastoral counselors in the American Association of Pastoral Counselors outnumbered women five to one at the two levels of certification for independent practice (Fellow and Diplomate). This was due in part to a continuing gender imbalance among ordained clergy more generally (women then represented only approximately 8 percent of all clergy in denominations ordaining women). Of all AAPC members, 87 percent were white (breakdown by Fellow and Diplomate not available), followed by 2 percent African American, 1.5 percent Asians, 0.4 percent Latinx, and less than 0.1 percent Native American, with a balance of approximately 9 percent “Other” or “Unlisted.” Data provided by AAPC office, March 21, 2003. While there were very few Latinx pastoral psychotherapists per se, there are many more Puerto Rican women active in pastoral ministries more generally, and in church leadership in the United States, especially in the Roman Catholic Church. See Corretjer-Nolla, “The Pastoral Care of the Puerto Rican Woman,” 72–73. While there has been some increase in spiritual caregivers/chaplains of color, research conducted during 2021 found that only ACPE (which now includes pastoral psychotherapists, as described below) and three other more ethnically diverse chaplaincy associations had resources for practitioners of color—see Wendy Cadge, Barbara Savage, and Marilyn J. D. Barnes, “Chaplains of Color: Histories and Practices,” Journal of Health Care Chaplaincy 29, no. 3 (2023): 245–255.



			 11. On issues of shame and dignity, see LaRiviere-Mestre, “Working with Latinos,” 50.



			 12. Latinx families have an 18 percent poverty rate (Pew, “U.S. Hispanic Population Living in Poverty, 2021,” https://www.pewresearch.org/chart/us-hispanics-­poverty/), which is greater than the US average. Over one-third of poor Latinx families are Puerto Rican. Corretjer-Nolla, “The Pastoral Care of the Puerto Rican Woman,” 79.



			 13. For a description of the positive cultural-social messages, practices, and traditions in the Latinx community, see LaRiviere-Mestre, “Working with Latinos,” 48–50.



			 14. I am using the term “difference” in a somewhat separate sense than that of Jacques Derrida in Positions, trans. Alan Bass (Chicago: University of Chicago Press, 1985), 39. For Derrida, the “play of differences” and his French term différance refers to the distance between signifiers and what they signify, in both time and space, erasing all essentialisms. I am using this term to mean both this slipperiness of verbal meaning and the phenomenologically felt differences among various groups as they are socially and culturally constructed through gender, race, class, and so forth.



			 15. Emmanuel Lartey, In Living Color; Lartey, “Pastoral Counselling in Multicultural Contexts,” in Counselling in Pastoral Settings, ed. Gordon Lynch (London and London: Routledge, 1999), 22–36; and Lartey, Pastoral Theology in an Intercultural World. For an overview of multicultural counseling theory, history, and practice, see, e.g., J. Manuel Casas, Lisa A. Suzuki, Charlene M. Alexander, and Margo A. Jackson, Handbook of Multicultural Counseling, 4th ed. (Los Angeles: Sage, 2017).



			 16. Ellen Ruderman, “Creative and Reparative Uses of Countertransference by Women Psychotherapists Treating Women Patients: A Clinical Research Study,” in The Psychology of Today’s Woman: New Psychoanalytic Visions, ed. T. Bernay and D. Cantor (Cambridge: Harvard University Press, 1986), 339–363.



			 17. Kohut, The Restoration of the Self (New York: International Universities Press, 1977); and Ernest Wolf, Treating the Self (New York: Guilford, 1988).



			 18. Corretjer-Nolla notes that indigenous Taino culture was matrilineal, and women in Puerto Rico have not been entirely subjugated even to the present day. A heritage of women’s strength thus lived on. However, systematic oppression of women, including sexual exploitation and constriction of roles and opportunities for women, was imposed on the culture by the Spanish conquistadors and missionaries. While Puerto Rican women in the United States are still disproportionately poor, divorced, and single mothers, increasing numbers of Puerto Rican women, especially in second and third generations in the United States, are achieving “higher education and better professional job opportunities.” Corretjer-Nolla, “The Pastoral Care of the Puerto Rican Woman,” 74.



			 19. Ivis LaRiviere-Mestre points out the distinction between being “strong” and being “tough,” in that survival is usually coupled with “the characteristic Puerto Rican gracious behavior of Puerto Rican women even when we encounter difficulties and undue pressures in our lives.” Personal communication, February 2003.

			 24. Sor Juana Inés de la Cruz is a fitting saintly Latina woman with whom Sara might identify. Sor Juana was “a Latina nun with a thirst for knowledge and learning and concern for the poor. She was a well-versed Mexican theologian who was told to stop her writing. She sold her private library and gave the money to the poor, since the church hierarchy had interpreted her intellectual abilities as a threat to the ‘status quo.’ If she refused, she would have faced the cruelty of the Inquisition.” Ivis LaRiviere-Mestre, personal communication, February 2003.

			 26. This term is used frequently in the survivor literature. It was originally used by Ernest Hemingway in A Farewell to Arms: “The world breaks everyone, and afterward many are strong at the broken places. But those that will not break it kills” (New York: Scribner’s Sons, 1995), 249.

Chapter Six


			 1. A new literature search (in 2024) discovered only one article, by Mark Chaves and Diana Garland, “The Prevalence of Clergy Sexual Advances Toward Adults in Their Congregations,” Journal for the Scientific Study of Religion, 48, no. 4 (2009): 817–824. This study was conducted using the 2008 wave of the online General Social Survey (GSS) database of the University of Chicago, with 3,559 randomly selected respondents, along with 92 follow-up phone interviews. The focus was on sexual misconduct in congregations. For an executive summary, see Diana R. Garland, “Executive Summary,” https://socialwork.web.baylor.edu/executive-summary. The authors requested a series of special codes to be added to the GSS in 2008 to measure the prevalence of various types of sexual misconduct by clergy. They found that “in the average American congregation of 400 persons, with women representing, on average, 60 percent of the congregation, there are, on average of 7 women who have experienced clergy sexual misconduct, and of the entire sample, 8 percent report having known about CSM occurring in a congregation they have attended. Therefore, in the average American congregation of 400 congregants, there are, on average, 32 persons who have experienced CSM in their community of faith.” (Garland, “Executive Summary”). The study found “no statistically significant differences in prevalence rates by region, religious tradition, current marital status, or current age.” (p. 821) There were statistically significant differences in education level (3.9 percent with less than a bachelor’s degree, vs. 1.3 percent with at least a bachelor’s degree), and by race (however with a very small number of respondents), with slightly more Black women experiencing sexual advances by clergy than white women. The focus of the survey questions is on congregational ministry. These particular data points were not used again in the GSS, and the study has not been replicated more recently (see https://gssnorc.org/. nd use search word “codebook,” to see questions used from 1972 to 2018). The most recent study regarding clergy misconduct, Christine Courtois and Arlene Lu Steinberg, “Sexual Boundary Violations in Pastoral Counseling,” in Sexual Boundary Violations in Psychotherapy: Facing Therapist Indiscretions, Transgressions, and Misconduct, ed. A. L. Steinberg, J. L. Alpert, and C. Courtois (Washington, DC: American Psychological Association, 2009), 157–183, is a somewhat limited overview of the issue, citing earlier statistics. The authors offer examples of Roman Catholic, Protestant, and Jewish communities—­however the category “Protestant” seems only to cover evangelical church bodies and makes no reference to the development of policies and safe church resources among “mainline Protestant” denominations such as the Evangelical Lutheran Church in America (ELCA), the Episcopal Church, the Presbyterian Church USA, the United Church of Christ, and the United Methodist Church. The article does not differentiate among congregational clergy and licensed/certified pastoral psychotherapists or counselors.

(Eugene, OR: Wipf and Stock, 2008).

Cooper-White, “The Use of the Self in Psychotherapy: A Comparative Study of Pastoral Counselors and Clinical Social Workers.”

			 8. p ≤ .001.



			 9. Male AAPC practitioners outnumbered women five to one at the Fellow and Diplomate levels at the time this research was conducted. Initial sample selection had to be corrected for gender imbalance. This was due in part to a gender imbalance in the ordained clergy more generally at the time (women represented only approximately 8 percent of all clergy in those denominations ordaining women). Nevertheless, a number of independent gender differences were found for which pastoral counseling vs. clinical social work discipline did not factor in as a significant covariable. (More recently, women still only represent 20.7% of (all) US clergy—Eileen Campbell-Reed, State of Clergywomen in the U.S.: A Statistical Update October 2018, https://eileencampbellreed.org/state-of-clergy/download-state-of-us-clergywomen/.)



			 10. Similar to findings by Ruderman, “Creative and Reparative Uses of Countertransference.”



			 11. Greenson, “The ‘Real’ Relationship.” Greenson’s concept of a “real relationship” assumes a classical definition of countertransference as distortions in perception of reality. The idea is that there is a reality-based relationship between therapist and patient that stands outside the transferential therapeutic relationship, and can be appealed to to guide the therapeutic process. This was a corrective to rigid classical psychoanalytic practice in which everything the patient says or does was considered to be 100 percent transferential neurosis, resistance, or otherwise unconsciously driven.

			 44. In a late paper, presented posthumously in Chicago in 1981, Kohut revisited his landmark 1959 paper on empathy, stating that both critics and supporters of self psychology had failed to grasp his concept of empathy. He reiterated that empathy is not some sort of telepathic activity or intuition, nor is it associated with any particular emotion such as compassion or affection. Kohut, “Introspection, Empathy and Psychoanalysis: An Examination of the Relationship Between Mode of Observation and Theory,” Journal of the American Psychoanalytic Association 7 (1959): 459–483; and Kohut, “Introspection, Empathy, and the Semi-Circle of Mental Health,” 395–407.



			 45. Kohut, “Introspection, Empathy, and the Semi-Circle of Mental Health,” 397.



			 46. Kohut, “Introspection, Empathy, and Psychoanalysis,” 459 passim; Patrick Casement uses a similar term, “trial identification,” in Learning from the Patient (New York: Guilford, 1985), originally from Robert Fleiss, “The Metapsychology of the Analyst,” Psychoanalytic Quarterly II (1942): 211–27.



			 47. Neville Symington, Narcissism, 40, 42, passim.



			 48. From a pastoral theological perspective, see P. Cooper-White, Many Voices (Minneapolis: Fortress Press, 2007), and Braided Selves (Eugene, OR: Cascade, 2011).



			 49. For example, Kohut, Analysis of the Self, 11 passim. For two different modern approaches to the concepts of multiplicity and a core self, see P. Cooper-White, “Interrogating Integration, Dissenting Dis-integration: Multiplicity as a Positive Metaphor in Therapy and Theology,” in P. Cooper-White, Braided Selves, 100–119; and Lisa Cataldo, “Multiple Selves, Multiple Gods? Functional Polytheism and the Postmodern Religious Patient,” Pastoral Psychology 57, nos. 1–2 (2008), 45–58. Donna Orange, who on ethical grounds does not subscribe to a multiple-self theory because she sees a core self as the foundation for ethical commitment, nevertheless quotes Kohut himself saying “‘I have come across the possibility—at least I take it very seriously—that there are individuals, and perhaps many more than we realize, who indeed have a number of selves and it is not an illness, not split personalities. These individuals can draw on different cohesive formations of themselves that exist on the surface and in depth and are independent from one another.’ (p. 76) . . . Sometimes he spoke of the self as ‘an important organizing center’ (p. 190). . . . Perhaps Kohut meant that the self (perhaps as agent) organizes experience, including the experience of personal multiplicity.”—Donna M. Orange, Review of Paul Tolpin and Marian Tolpin, eds., The Chicago Institute Lectures: Kohut (Hillsdale, NJ: Analytic Press, 1996), Psychoanalytic Psychology 17 (2000): 420–431. Orange and I have debated multiple vs. core self theories (Cooper-White, “Multiplicity, Intersubjectivity, and Recognizing the Other: Empathy as a Bridge to Justice,” with respondent Donna Orange, Psychology and the Other Conference, Cambridge, MA, October 9–11, 2015). While we disagree on the relational understanding of multiplicity of the self vs. the presupposition of a core self, we agree on the primacy of ethics and that in all clinical circumstances there must be space for multiple meanings to emerge.



			 50. Jung, C.G. (1980). The archetypes and the collective unconscious, 2nd ed. In Collected Works of C.G. Jung, vol. 9, part 1, ed. and trans. Gerhard Adler and R. F.C. Hull (Princeton, NJ: Princeton University Press, 1981).





Chapter Seven


			 1. Graham Ward, “Introduction, or, A Guide to Theological Thinking in Cyberspace,” in The Postmodern God: A Theological Reader (Malden, MA: Blackwell, 1997), xv.



			 2. John Polkinghorne, Quarks, Chaos and Christianity: Questions to Science and Religion (New York: Crossroad, 1997).



			 3. For further elaboration in response to this question, see P. 			 4. As Johnson herself comprehensively describes in She Who Is (New York: Crossroad, 1994), 210, there are numerous twentieth-century articulations of trinitarian theology, many of which could be considered relational. For example, Jürgen Moltmann’s influential idea of the Trinity as “divine society,” The Trinity and the Kingdom, trans. M. Kohl (San Francisco: Harper and Row, 1981); John Macquarrie’s rendering of the Trinity as primordial source, expressive dynamism, and unitive Being in Love, Principles of Christian Theology, 2nd ed. (New York: Scribner’s Sons, 1977), 190–210; Sallie McFague’s feminist trinity of mother-lover-friend, Models of God (Philadelphia, PA: Fortress, 1987), 35 passim. For a much more in-depth exploration of trinitarian theology, multiplicity, and further images for the multiplicity of God, see P. Cooper-White, Many Voices, 67–94, also summarized in P. Cooper-White, “Dancing Partners: Trinitarian Theology and the Use of the Self in Pastoral Care and Counseling,” in P. Cooper-White Braided Selves, 120–134.

			 11. Milbank’s trinitarian formulations may be understood within a larger trend in contemporary Anglican theology of understanding the Trinity as community, e.g., S. H. Ford, “Perichoresis and Interpretation: Samuel Taylor Coleridge’s Trinitarian Concept of Unity,” Theology 89, no. 727 (1986): 20–23; British Council of Churches, The Forgotten Trinity: The Report of the BCC Study Commission on Trinitarian Doctrine Today (London: BCC, 1989); J. O’Donnell, “The Trinity as Divine Community,” Gregorianum 69, no. 1 (1988): 5–74; and Catherine Mowry LaCugna and J. O’Donnell, “Returning from ‘the Far Country’: Theses for a Contemporary Trinitarian Theology,” Scottish Journal of Theology 41 (1988): 191–215, reviewed in Jürgen Moltmann, The Trinity and the Kingdom, xii.



			 12. John Milbank, “Postmodern Critical Augustinianism: A Short Summa in Forty-two Responses to Unasked Questions,” in Ward, ed., The Postmodern God (Oxford: Blackwell, 1997), 274.



			 13. Milbank, “Postmodern Critical Augustinianism,” 267.



			 14. Milbank, “Postmodern Critical Augustinianism,” 268–269. This idea echoes earlier formulations of the social trinity; cf. Moltmann, The Trinity and the Kingdom; and Boff, Holy Trinity, Perfect Community, esp. 118–119. In Milbank’s attempt to work within a postmodern framework, a subtle shift has occurred in which the implicit ethic is not justice based on equality (cf. Luce Irigaray’s critique, “Equal to Whom?” in Differences: A Journal of Feminist Cultural Studies 1, no. 2 [1989]: 59–76, reprinted in Ward, The Postmodern God, 198–214) as much as a nonviolence based on valuing differences. For a further discussion of Irigaray’s critique of equality, see also Susan Jones, “This God Who Is Not One: Irigaray and Barth on the Divine,” in Transfigurations: Theology and the French Feminists, ed. C. Kim, S. St. Ville, and S. Simonaitis (Minneapolis: Fortress, 1993), 109–141; and Grace Jantzen, “Luce Irigaray: Introduction,” in Ward, The Postmodern God, 191–197.



			 15. Milbank’s theology imports one of the central problems of postmodernist philosophy—that of ethical criteria. In his own words, “Postmodernism claims to refuse dialectic, but this is the instance of its failure to do so; it is right to make the effort” (“Postmodern Critical Augustinianism,” 270). Like many postmodern writers, Milbank links violence with exclusion of the other, but he does not resolve the real social and political problems resulting from the inclusion even of oppressors (e.g., p. 273).



			 16. Milbank, “Postmodern Critical Augustinianism,” 275. This line of thought draws in part on Gilles Deleuze, e.g., The Logic of Sense, trans. Mark Lester and Charles Stivale (New York: Columbia University Press, 1990); Difference and Repetition, trans. Paul Patton (New York: Columbia University Press, 1994); and Deleuze and Félix Guattari, A Thousand Plateaus, trans. Brian Massumi (London: Athlone, 1988).



			 17. Cf., Milbank, “Postmodern Critical Augustinianism,” 274–275.



			 18. Irigaray, An Ethics of Sexual Difference, 7, also cited in Ward, “Introduction,” The Postmodern God, xxvi.



			 19. Milbank, “Postmodern Critical Augustinianism,” 274.



			 20. First proposed by Mary Daly in Beyond God the Father: Toward a Philosophy of Women’s Liberation (Boston: Beacon, 1985), 34–35; also in Virginia Ramey Mollenkott, Godding: Human Responsibility and the Bible (New York: Crossroad, 1987).



			 21. See, for example, Marjorie Suchocki, God Christ Church: A Practical Guide to Process Theology (New York: Crossroad, 1982); and John Cobb, Christ in a Pluralistic Age (Philadelphia, PA: Westminster, 1975). I am using process theology selectively. Certain themes in process theology, especially a tendency toward binary formulations, are not so useful in constructing a theological model of multiplicity—e.g., Alfred North Whitehead’s polarities of conceptual and physical, and God’s “primordial nature” and “consequent nature,” part 5 in Process and Reality: An Essay in Cosmology (New York: Free Press, 1978; orig. publ. 1929), diagrammed by Suchocki as a yin-yang in God Christ Church, 113. See also John Cobb’s discussion of “binity” in “Relativization of the Trinity,” in Trinity in Process: A Relational Theology of God, ed. Joseph Bracken and Majorie Suchocki (New York: Continuum, 1997), 1–22. Such language in process theology as God’s “feeling of the world” (Cobb, “Relativization of the Trinity”) also—perhaps—ascribes an unnecessarily monistic and anthropomorphic subjectivity to God. Whitehead’s category of subjective unity of “actual entities,” including God (part 3), does not encourage a normative model of multiplicity.
